================================================================Question 1
Question ID: foundations-ambiguity:A1
Paired Question ID: foundations-ambiguity:B1
Competency ID: pattern-ambiguity
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What does this module say about some patterns at the code level?

OPTIONS:
A. All patterns look unique in code
B. Some patterns look almost identical in structure
C. Patterns never share code shapes
D. Ambiguity is always a defect

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Some patterns look almost identical in structure

RATIONALE:
Some patterns share nearly identical structure (e.g., Builder vs Method Chaining), so ambiguity is inherent — not a defect.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: foundations-ambiguity:A2
Paired Question ID: foundations-ambiguity:B2
Competency ID: pattern-ambiguity
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Why do Builder and Method Chaining look similar in code?

OPTIONS:
A. Both are creational only
B. Both rely on inheritance
C. Both use fluent method chaining (return *this)
D. Both are singletons

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Both use fluent method chaining (return *this)

RATIONALE:
Both express a fluent interface returning *this, so their surface code looks alike though their intent differs.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: foundations-ambiguity:A3
Paired Question ID: foundations-ambiguity:B3
Competency ID: pattern-ambiguity
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Two classes each hold a member of the same interface and forward calls to it. To tell which pattern, you should examine ___.

OPTIONS:
A. the file size
B. the intent — e.g. controlling access vs adding behaviour vs adapting an interface
C. only the variable names
D. the compiler output

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. the intent — e.g. controlling access vs adding behaviour vs adapting an interface

RATIONALE:
Wrappers that forward to a same-interface member are distinguished by intent (control access vs add behaviour vs adapt), not by size, names, or compiler output.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns, ch. 1

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: foundations-ambiguity:A4
Paired Question ID: foundations-ambiguity:B4
Competency ID: pattern-ambiguity
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Adapter, Decorator, and Proxy all wrap a held member. Which cue best separates Proxy from Decorator?

OPTIONS:
A. Proxy adds new responsibilities while Decorator restricts access
B. They cannot be distinguished
C. Proxy controls access to a same-interface subject; Decorator augments behaviour through the same interface
D. A Proxy must be abstract

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Proxy controls access to a same-interface subject; Decorator augments behaviour through the same interface

RATIONALE:
Proxy’s intent is controlling access to the subject (same interface); Decorator’s intent is augmenting behaviour. That intent difference is the cue.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns, ch. 1

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: foundations-ambiguity:A5
Paired Question ID: foundations-ambiguity:B5
Competency ID: pattern-ambiguity
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Given two near-identical implementations, which is the most defensible way to resolve the pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Pick whichever name sounds best
B. Always choose Decorator
C. Flip a coin
D. Resolve by the intent shown in naming, collaborators, and call-site shape

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Resolve by the intent shown in naming, collaborators, and call-site shape

RATIONALE:
The defensible method weighs intent evidence (lexemes, collaborators, call-site shape) rather than guessing or defaulting.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

QUESTION:
Which statement reflects this module’s honest claim?

OPTIONS:
A. Structural ambiguity between some patterns is inherent
B. Every pattern is structurally unique
C. Ambiguity means the code is wrong
D. Patterns are never similar

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Structural ambiguity between some patterns is inherent

RATIONALE:
The module’s claim is that structural ambiguity between some patterns is inherent, not a sign of error.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

Question 2
Question ID: foundations-ambiguity:B2
Paired Question ID: foundations-ambiguity:A2
Competency ID: pattern-ambiguity
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Why can two patterns share the same class diagram?

OPTIONS:
A. Because diagrams are always inaccurate
B. Because structure alone does not capture intent, which differs
C. Because they are the same pattern
D. Because UML forbids uniqueness

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Because structure alone does not capture intent, which differs

RATIONALE:
A class diagram shows structure but not intent; two patterns with the same structure differ in purpose.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: foundations-ambiguity:B3
Paired Question ID: foundations-ambiguity:A3
Competency ID: pattern-ambiguity
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A class implements an interface, holds a same-interface member, forwards calls, and adds logging around them. Which intent is most likely?

OPTIONS:
A. Adapting an incompatible interface
B. Ensuring a single instance
C. Adding behaviour transparently (decoration)
D. Creating families of related products

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Adding behaviour transparently (decoration)

RATIONALE:
Forwarding through the same interface while adding behaviour (logging) signals Decorator intent, not adaptation, singleton, or factory.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns, ch. 1

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: foundations-ambiguity:B4
Paired Question ID: foundations-ambiguity:A4
Competency ID: pattern-ambiguity
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What evidence best distinguishes Adapter from Decorator?

OPTIONS:
A. They are identical
B. Adapter targets a different/incompatible interface; Decorator keeps the same interface and adds behaviour
C. Decorator changes the interface
D. Adapter exists to add logging

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Adapter targets a different/incompatible interface; Decorator keeps the same interface and adds behaviour

RATIONALE:
Adapter converts to a different/incompatible interface; Decorator keeps the same interface and augments behaviour — the defining difference.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns, ch. 1

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: foundations-ambiguity:B5
Paired Question ID: foundations-ambiguity:A5
Competency ID: pattern-ambiguity
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Which approach to resolving a near-tie between two patterns is most academically defensible?

OPTIONS:
A. Guess from the class name
B. Always pick the first catalog match
C. Ignore the tie entirely
D. Weigh intent signals and accept genuine ambiguity rather than forcing a single label

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Weigh intent signals and accept genuine ambiguity rather than forcing a single label

RATIONALE:
Weighing intent signals and acknowledging genuine ambiguity is defensible; guessing, defaulting, or ignoring the tie is not.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

QUESTION:
Which answer best names the main idea in Ambiguity?

OPTIONS:
A. Ambiguity gives a named, reusable way to solve the module's design problem.
B. Ambiguity is only a compiler optimization flag.
C. Ambiguity means every class must inherit from one base class.
D. Ambiguity replaces the need to read code structure.

CORRECT ANSWER: A. Ambiguity gives a named, reusable way to solve the module's design problem.

QUESTION:
Which pair genuinely shares a structural shape?

OPTIONS:
A. Builder and Method Chaining, which both rely on `return *this`.
B. Singleton and Observer, which share no structure.
C. Factory and Iterator, which are identical.

QUESTION:
When two patterns fit the same class, the detector should…

OPTIONS:
A. Silently pick one and hide the other.
B. Surface both and ask the human — ambiguity is information, not failure.

D. Rewrite the source to remove ambiguity.

MODULE ID: foundations-beginner-mistakes

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: foundations-beginner-mistakes:A1
Paired Question ID: foundations-beginner-mistakes:B1
Competency ID: pattern-misuse-awareness
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Which is a common beginner mistake with design patterns?

OPTIONS:
A. Reading the catalog carefully
B. Forcing a pattern where the underlying problem does not exist
C. Writing unit tests
D. Using version control

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Forcing a pattern where the underlying problem does not exist

RATIONALE:
Over-applying a pattern where its problem is absent is a classic trap; the other options are good practices.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: foundations-beginner-mistakes:A2
Paired Question ID: foundations-beginner-mistakes:B2
Competency ID: pattern-misuse-awareness
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Why is naming a class after a pattern not evidence the pattern is implemented?

OPTIONS:
A. Because names are case-sensitive
B. Because the pattern must be in a separate file
C. Because a name is only a label; structure and intent determine the pattern
D. Because compilers reject pattern names

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Because a name is only a label; structure and intent determine the pattern

RATIONALE:
A name is just a label; the pattern exists only if the structure/intent are present. The other options are incorrect.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: foundations-beginner-mistakes:B1
Paired Question ID: foundations-beginner-mistakes:A1
Competency ID: pattern-misuse-awareness
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Over-applying design patterns most often leads to ___.

OPTIONS:
A. faster code
B. fewer source files
C. guaranteed correctness
D. unnecessary complexity and indirection

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. unnecessary complexity and indirection

RATIONALE:
Forcing patterns adds indirection/complexity without benefit; it does not improve speed, file count, or correctness.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: foundations-beginner-mistakes:B2
Paired Question ID: foundations-beginner-mistakes:A2
Competency ID: pattern-misuse-awareness
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A learner adds “Factory” to a class name to “use the pattern.” What is the flaw?

OPTIONS:
A. The name should be lowercase
B. Renaming does not create the pattern’s structure or intent
C. Factories must be singletons
D. Patterns cannot be named at all

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Renaming does not create the pattern’s structure or intent

RATIONALE:
Renaming alone does not create the required structure/intent, so the pattern is not implemented. The other options are wrong.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

QUESTION:
Why is making classes depend heavily on each other a trap?

OPTIONS:
A. Tight coupling is always faster at runtime.
B. Loose connections are easier to change and maintain; tight coupling resists change.
C. The compiler rejects loosely coupled code.
D. Coupling has no effect on maintenance.

CORRECT ANSWER: B. Loose connections are easier to change and maintain; tight coupling resists change.

QUESTION:
What is the most common beginner mistake with design patterns?

OPTIONS:
A. Refusing to use any patterns at all.
B. Forcing a pattern onto a problem that does not need it ("pattern fever").
C. Always preferring composition over inheritance.
D. Reading too many books.

CORRECT ANSWER: B. Forcing a pattern onto a problem that does not need it ("pattern fever").

CATEGORY: FOUNDATIONS
MODULE: Three main pattern categories
MODULE ID: foundations-categories
ASSESSMENT TYPE: Formal Pre-Test — Form A
APPLICABLE BLOOM LEVELS: Remember, Understand
NON-APPLICABLE LEVELS: Apply, Analyze, Evaluate, Create
================================================================Question 1
Question ID: foundations-categories:A1
Paired Question ID: foundations-categories:B1
Competency ID: pattern-families
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Which trio names the three classic Gang-of-Four pattern families?

OPTIONS:
A. Compiling, Linking, Running
B. Frontend, Backend, Database
C. Creational, Structural, Behavioural
D. Static, Dynamic, Virtual

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Creational, Structural, Behavioural

RATIONALE:
The GoF families are Creational, Structural, and Behavioural. The other groupings are build phases, app tiers, and binding kinds — not pattern families.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns, ch. 1

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: foundations-categories:A2
Paired Question ID: foundations-categories:B2
Competency ID: pattern-families
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A pattern’s main job is deciding HOW objects are created. Which family is it in?

OPTIONS:
A. Creational
B. Structural
C. Behavioural
D. None — creation is never a pattern concern

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Creational

RATIONALE:
Creational patterns address object creation/instantiation concerns. Structural deals with composition; Behavioural with communication; creation is squarely a Creational concern.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns, ch. 1

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: foundations-categories:A3
Paired Question ID: foundations-categories:B3
Competency ID: pattern-families
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Which sentence correctly maps the three families by intent?

OPTIONS:
A. Creational connects objects; Structural decides communication; Behavioural makes objects
B. Creational makes objects; Structural composes/connects them; Behavioural decides how they communicate
C. All three families do the same thing under different names
D. Structural is a special case of Creational

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Creational makes objects; Structural composes/connects them; Behavioural decides how they communicate

RATIONALE:
Creational = how objects are made; Structural = how they are composed/related; Behavioural = how responsibilities/communication are organised. The other options swap or conflate these intents.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns, ch. 1

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: foundations-categories:B1
Paired Question ID: foundations-categories:A1
Competency ID: pattern-families
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
The Gang-of-Four catalogue groups patterns into three families. Which option lists them?

OPTIONS:
A. Behavioural, Structural, Creational
B. Public, Private, Protected
C. Stack, Heap, Register
D. Model, View, Controller

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Behavioural, Structural, Creational

RATIONALE:
The three GoF families are Creational, Structural, and Behavioural (order aside). Access specifiers, memory regions, and MVC are unrelated groupings.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns, ch. 1

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: foundations-categories:B2
Paired Question ID: foundations-categories:A2
Competency ID: pattern-families
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A pattern primarily organises how responsibilities are assigned and how objects talk at runtime. Which family?

OPTIONS:
A. Creational
B. Structural
C. Behavioural
D. None — communication is not a pattern concern

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Behavioural

RATIONALE:
Behavioural patterns concern responsibility assignment and runtime communication between objects. Creational is about making objects; Structural about composing them.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns, ch. 1

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: foundations-categories:B3
Paired Question ID: foundations-categories:A3
Competency ID: pattern-families
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Which mapping of family-to-intent is correct?

OPTIONS:
A. Structural makes objects; Behavioural composes them; Creational handles communication
B. All families address object creation only
C. Behavioural is a subset of Structural
D. Creational = object creation; Structural = composition/relationships; Behavioural = communication/responsibility

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Creational = object creation; Structural = composition/relationships; Behavioural = communication/responsibility

RATIONALE:
Only the last option maps each family to its real intent. The others swap intents, over-narrow to creation, or invent a subset relationship.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns, ch. 1

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: foundations-code-structure:A1
Paired Question ID: foundations-code-structure:B1
Competency ID: code-structure-analysis
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What does CodiNeo’s analyser inspect to detect patterns?

OPTIONS:
A. Only the code comments
B. The program’s runtime speed
C. Class relationships and structure via a parse tree (AST)
D. The source file names

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Class relationships and structure via a parse tree (AST)

RATIONALE:
Detection works over class relationships/structure captured in a parse tree (AST), not comments, runtime speed, or file names.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: foundations-code-structure:A2
Paired Question ID: foundations-code-structure:B2
Competency ID: code-structure-analysis
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Why can patterns be detected from code structure rather than from names?

OPTIONS:
A. Because every pattern class is named after its pattern
B. Because patterns are defined by relationships and responsibilities, not identifiers
C. Because the compiler emits pattern tags
D. Because comments declare the pattern

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Because patterns are defined by relationships and responsibilities, not identifiers

RATIONALE:
Patterns are defined by relationships/responsibilities, so structure is the real evidence; names, compilers, and comments are not reliable signals.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: foundations-code-structure:B1
Paired Question ID: foundations-code-structure:A1
Competency ID: code-structure-analysis
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Which representation lets a tool analyse class relationships and dependencies?

OPTIONS:
A. A rendered screenshot of the code
B. An abstract syntax tree (parse tree) of the source
C. A list of file sizes
D. The commit history

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. An abstract syntax tree (parse tree) of the source

RATIONALE:
An abstract syntax tree (parse tree) exposes structure/relationships for analysis; the other options carry no structural information.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: foundations-code-structure:B2
Paired Question ID: foundations-code-structure:A2
Competency ID: code-structure-analysis
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A class is named “ObserverManager” but never notifies dependents. Why won’t a structure-based analyser tag it as Observer?

OPTIONS:
A. Because the name is misspelled
B. Because Observer must live in its own file
C. Because “Manager” classes can never be patterns
D. Because detection relies on structural evidence, not the class name

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Because detection relies on structural evidence, not the class name

RATIONALE:
Structure-based detection requires the observer/notify relationship; the name alone is not evidence, so the class is not tagged.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

QUESTION:
Which answer best names the main idea in Code Structure?

OPTIONS:
A. Code Structure gives a named, reusable way to solve the module's design problem.
B. Code Structure is only a compiler optimization flag.
C. Code Structure means every class must inherit from one base class.
D. Code Structure replaces the need to read code structure.

CORRECT ANSWER: A. Code Structure gives a named, reusable way to solve the module's design problem.

Question 4
Question ID: foundations-code-structure:analyzing
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 3
Bloom Level: Analyzing
Item Type: mcq

QUESTION:
Why do pattern detectors walk an AST instead of grep-searching source text?

OPTIONS:
A. Because grep is too slow.
B. Because code is a tree; patterns are shapes in that tree, and matching needs structural context.
C. Because AST parsers are required by ISO C++.
D. Because text search is forbidden on Linux.

CORRECT ANSWER: B. Because code is a tree; patterns are shapes in that tree, and matching needs structural context.

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: foundations-code-structure:evaluating
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 4
Bloom Level: Evaluating
Item Type: mcq (GENERATED FALLBACK — excluded from formal assessment)

QUESTION:
Which review comment best evaluates a Code Structure implementation?

OPTIONS:
A. It should show the code structure intent clearly, not only coincidental syntax.
B. It passes automatically if the class name contains Pattern.
C. It is correct when every method is static.
D. It should remove all interfaces to reduce files.

CORRECT ANSWER: A. It should show the code structure intent clearly, not only coincidental syntax.

CATEGORY: FOUNDATIONS
MODULE: The connotative-definition rule
MODULE ID: foundations-connotative-definition
ASSESSMENT TYPE: Formal Pre-Test — Form A
APPLICABLE BLOOM LEVELS: Remember, Understand
NON-APPLICABLE LEVELS: Apply, Analyze, Evaluate, Create
================================================================Question 1
Question ID: foundations-connotative-definition:A1
Paired Question ID: foundations-connotative-definition:B1
Competency ID: connotative-definition
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
According to this module, the way to reduce pattern ambiguity is to ___.

OPTIONS:
A. guess the most likely pattern
B. add context to the definition itself (as connotation adds to denotation)
C. rename the class
D. remove the abstraction

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. add context to the definition itself (as connotation adds to denotation)

RATIONALE:
The rule reduces ambiguity by adding contextual intent to the definition, mirroring connotation in language — not guessing, renaming, or removing abstraction.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: foundations-connotative-definition:A2
Paired Question ID: foundations-connotative-definition:B2
Competency ID: connotative-definition
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What does the denotation-vs-connotation analogy imply for pattern definitions?

OPTIONS:
A. Definitions should simply be shorter
B. Patterns need no definition
C. A pattern’s meaning sharpens when intent/context is added to its bare structure
D. Structure alone is always sufficient

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. A pattern’s meaning sharpens when intent/context is added to its bare structure

RATIONALE:
Just as connotation adds meaning beyond denotation, adding intent/context to bare structure sharpens a pattern’s definition.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

QUESTION:
To reduce ambiguity, the connotative-definition rule says you should ___.

OPTIONS:
A. add contextual intent to the definition rather than guess
B. pick the shortest pattern name
C. delete the ambiguous code
D. avoid interfaces

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. add contextual intent to the definition rather than guess

RATIONALE:
The rule adds contextual intent to the definition rather than guessing; the alternatives do not reduce ambiguity.

* CodiNeo Foundations module content

QUESTION:
How does CodiNeo apply the connotative-definition rule?

OPTIONS:
A. By ignoring intent entirely
B. By using only class names
C. By trusting code comments
D. By enriching a pattern’s structural definition with intent/context cues

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. By enriching a pattern’s structural definition with intent/context cues

RATIONALE:
CodiNeo enriches the structural definition with intent/context cues so detection is less ambiguous; the other options ignore intent.

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: foundations-context-variation:A1
Paired Question ID: foundations-context-variation:B1
Competency ID: convention-variation
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Two teams that both use Builder may ___.

OPTIONS:
A. produce byte-identical code
B. write code that looks different while following the same intent
C. never compile
D. always disagree on the result

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. write code that looks different while following the same intent

RATIONALE:
Teams can implement the same pattern with different surface conventions while keeping the same intent.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: foundations-context-variation:A2
Paired Question ID: foundations-context-variation:B2
Competency ID: convention-variation
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How does CodiNeo handle convention variation between teams?

OPTIONS:
A. It picks one team’s style as universal truth
B. It rejects all variation
C. It standardises on detectable structural evidence rather than one team’s convention
D. It ignores structure

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. It standardises on detectable structural evidence rather than one team’s convention

RATIONALE:
CodiNeo standardises on detectable structural evidence rather than crowning one team’s convention, so reasonable variation is tolerated.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

QUESTION:
What is the module’s point about coding conventions?

OPTIONS:
A. The same pattern can be written different ways across teams
B. Every team writes identical code
C. Conventions never vary
D. Variation always indicates a bug

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. The same pattern can be written different ways across teams

RATIONALE:
The point is that the same pattern can be written differently across teams; the alternatives are false.

OPTIONS:
A. forces one convention on everyone
B. bans alternative styles
C. requires identical formatting
D. detects the pattern by structural evidence, allowing surface variation

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. detects the pattern by structural evidence, allowing surface variation

RATIONALE:
It detects patterns by structural evidence and allows surface variation, instead of forcing or banning styles.

================================================================
CATEGORY: FOUNDATIONS
MODULE: Program to an interface
MODULE ID: foundations-interface-principle
ASSESSMENT TYPE: Formal Pre-Test — Form A
APPLICABLE BLOOM LEVELS: Remember, Understand, Apply
NON-APPLICABLE LEVELS: Analyze, Evaluate, Create
================================================================Question 1
Question ID: foundations-interface-principle:A1
Paired Question ID: foundations-interface-principle:B1
Competency ID: interface-principle
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What does “program to an interface, not an implementation” mean?

OPTIONS:
A. Always use abstract classes for everything
B. Write all code in header files
C. Depend on a shared abstraction instead of one specific concrete class
D. Avoid interfaces to reduce the file count

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Depend on a shared abstraction instead of one specific concrete class

RATIONALE:
The principle says to depend on a shared abstraction (capability) rather than a specific concrete class. It is not about abstract classes everywhere, header files, or avoiding interfaces.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns, ch. 1

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: foundations-interface-principle:A2
Paired Question ID: foundations-interface-principle:B2
Competency ID: interface-principle
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Why does depending on an abstraction make a system easier to change?

OPTIONS:
A. It always reduces the number of classes
B. Callers are decoupled from concrete types, so implementations can be swapped without editing callers
C. It makes the program run faster
D. It removes the need for tests

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Callers are decoupled from concrete types, so implementations can be swapped without editing callers

RATIONALE:
Decoupling callers from concrete types lets implementations be substituted without changing the callers. It does not inherently reduce classes, improve speed, or remove tests.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: foundations-interface-principle:A3
Paired Question ID: foundations-interface-principle:B3
Competency ID: interface-principle
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A team must support several payment providers and switch between them without changing checkout code. Which approach applies the principle?

OPTIONS:
A. Inline every provider into the checkout code
B. Store the active provider in a global variable
C. Hard-code one default provider
D. Have checkout depend on a payment abstraction that each provider implements

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Have checkout depend on a payment abstraction that each provider implements

RATIONALE:
Depending on a payment abstraction implemented by each provider lets checkout stay unchanged while providers vary — the principle in action. The other options couple checkout to concretes.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

------------------------------------------------

Question ID: foundations-interface-principle:B1
Paired Question ID: foundations-interface-principle:A1
Competency ID: interface-principle
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
“Program to an interface” tells you to depend on ___.

OPTIONS:
A. a shared abstraction rather than a specific concrete class
B. the most-derived class available
C. only static free functions
D. the standard library

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. a shared abstraction rather than a specific concrete class

RATIONALE:
The rule is to depend on a shared abstraction rather than a specific concrete class. The other options misstate it.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns, ch. 1

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: foundations-interface-principle:B2
Paired Question ID: foundations-interface-principle:A2
Competency ID: interface-principle
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A module depends on an abstraction rather than a concrete class. What does this most enable?

OPTIONS:
A. Guaranteed lower memory use
B. Fewer source files in all cases
C. The ability to substitute implementations without changing the dependent module
D. Automatic multithreading

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. The ability to substitute implementations without changing the dependent module

RATIONALE:
An abstraction dependency enables substituting implementations without editing the dependent module. It gives no memory, file-count, or threading guarantee.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: foundations-interface-principle:B3
Paired Question ID: foundations-interface-principle:A3
Competency ID: interface-principle
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A logging component must write to console, file, or network sinks chosen at runtime without changing callers. Which approach applies the principle?

OPTIONS:
A. Duplicate the logger for each sink
B. Depend on a sink abstraction that each backend implements
C. Put a large switch statement in every caller
D. Make every sink a global object

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Depend on a sink abstraction that each backend implements

RATIONALE:
Depending on a sink abstraction implemented by each backend lets the sink vary at runtime without changing callers. The alternatives couple callers to concretes.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

QUESTION:
Which patterns rest directly on this principle?

OPTIONS:
A. Strategy, Factory, Adapter, and Bridge.
B. Only Singleton.
C. No patterns — it is unrelated to patterns.
D. Only Creational patterns.

CORRECT ANSWER: A. Strategy, Factory, Adapter, and Bridge.

CATEGORY: FOUNDATIONS
MODULE: OOP foundations
MODULE ID: foundations-oop
ASSESSMENT TYPE: Formal Pre-Test — Form A
APPLICABLE BLOOM LEVELS: Remember, Understand
NON-APPLICABLE LEVELS: Apply, Analyze, Evaluate, Create
================================================================Question 1
Question ID: foundations-oop:A1
Paired Question ID: foundations-oop:B1
Competency ID: oop-foundations
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Which OOP mechanism lets one interface call be served by different concrete implementations at runtime?

OPTIONS:
A. Encapsulation
B. Polymorphism
C. Macro expansion
D. Static linking

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Polymorphism

RATIONALE:
Polymorphism (via virtual dispatch) lets a call through a base interface run different concrete behaviour. Encapsulation hides state; macros and linking are unrelated build-time mechanisms.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: foundations-oop:A2
Paired Question ID: foundations-oop:B2
Competency ID: oop-foundations
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Why do many design patterns depend on programming to an interface (abstraction) rather than a concrete class?

OPTIONS:
A. It lets implementations vary independently of the code that uses them
B. It always reduces the number of classes
C. It makes the program use less memory
D. It removes the need for constructors

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. It lets implementations vary independently of the code that uses them

RATIONALE:
Depending on an abstraction decouples clients from concrete types, so implementations can change/substitute without touching callers — the basis of many patterns. It does not inherently reduce classes/memory or remove constructors.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns, ch. 1

------------------------------------------------

================================================================
CATEGORY: FOUNDATIONS
MODULE: OOP foundations
MODULE ID: foundations-oop
ASSESSMENT TYPE: Formal Post-Test — Form B
APPLICABLE BLOOM LEVELS: Remember, Understand
NON-APPLICABLE LEVELS: Apply, Analyze, Evaluate, Create
================================================================Question 1
Question ID: foundations-oop:B1
Paired Question ID: foundations-oop:A1
Competency ID: oop-foundations
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A base-class pointer invokes a method and the derived class’s version runs. Which OOP concept is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Static binding
B. Encapsulation
C. Polymorphism
D. Preprocessing

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Polymorphism

RATIONALE:
Dispatching to the derived override through a base pointer is runtime polymorphism. Static binding resolves at compile time; encapsulation and preprocessing are different concepts.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: foundations-oop:B2
Paired Question ID: foundations-oop:A2
Competency ID: oop-foundations
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What does depending on an abstraction (interface) buy a design that depending on a concrete class does not?

OPTIONS:
A. Guaranteed lower memory use
B. Substitutable implementations without changing client code
C. Fewer total source files in every case
D. Automatic thread safety

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Substitutable implementations without changing client code

RATIONALE:
An interface dependency lets concrete implementations be swapped without editing clients — enabling extension and testing. It gives no memory, file-count, or thread-safety guarantee.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns, ch. 1

Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
When two patterns share the same class diagram, what do you use to tell them apart?

OPTIONS:
A. File size
B. Intent
C. Line count
D. Author name

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Intent

RATIONALE:
Identical structure is disambiguated by intent; size, line count, and author are irrelevant.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: foundations-same-structure:A2
Paired Question ID: foundations-same-structure:B2
Competency ID: intent-over-structure
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Why is intent, rather than structure, the deciding factor when diagrams match?

OPTIONS:
A. Structure is hard to read
B. Intent descriptions are shorter
C. Identical structures can serve different purposes, so intent disambiguates
D. Intent is required by the compiler

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Identical structures can serve different purposes, so intent disambiguates

RATIONALE:
Because the same structure can serve different purposes, the differentiator must be intent — not readability, length, or the compiler.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

Intent surfaces in lexemes (naming), collaborators, and call-site shape — signals an analyser can read statically.

CATEGORY: FOUNDATIONS
MODULE: What is a design pattern?
MODULE ID: foundations-what-is-pattern
ASSESSMENT TYPE: Formal Pre-Test — Form A
APPLICABLE BLOOM LEVELS: Remember, Understand, Apply
NON-APPLICABLE LEVELS: Analyze, Evaluate, Create
================================================================Question 1
Question ID: foundations-what-is-pattern:A1
Paired Question ID: foundations-what-is-pattern:B1
Competency ID: pattern-definition
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: pilot-reviewed

QUESTION:
What is a design pattern, most precisely?

OPTIONS:
A. A named, reusable solution to a recurring object-oriented design problem
B. A language keyword that enables polymorphism
C. A base class that every program must extend
D. A tool that automatically formats source code

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. A named, reusable solution to a recurring object-oriented design problem

RATIONALE:
A design pattern is a named, reusable solution to a recurring OO design problem. It is not a language keyword, a mandatory base class, or a formatting tool.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns, ch. 1

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: foundations-what-is-pattern:A2
Paired Question ID: foundations-what-is-pattern:B2
Competency ID: pattern-captures
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: pilot-reviewed

QUESTION:
A design pattern primarily captures which of the following?

OPTIONS:
A. A compiler optimization flag
B. A file-naming convention
C. A proven structure-and-intent for a recurring design problem
D. A unit-test framework

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. A proven structure-and-intent for a recurring design problem

RATIONALE:
A pattern captures a proven structure and intent for a recurring problem — not a compiler flag, a naming convention, or a testing framework.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns, ch. 1

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: foundations-what-is-pattern:A3
Paired Question ID: foundations-what-is-pattern:B3
Competency ID: pattern-value-communication
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: pilot-reviewed

QUESTION:
Why does giving a recurring design a shared name help a team?

OPTIONS:
A. It makes the program run faster
B. One word can convey a whole structure and intent, reducing explanation
C. It removes the need for any comments
D. It guarantees the code compiles

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. One word can convey a whole structure and intent, reducing explanation

RATIONALE:
A shared name lets one term convey a whole structure/intent, shortening communication. It has no effect on runtime speed, comments, or compilation.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns, ch. 1

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: foundations-what-is-pattern:A4
Paired Question ID: foundations-what-is-pattern:B4
Competency ID: pattern-vs-other-concepts
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: pilot-reviewed

QUESTION:
How does a design pattern differ from an algorithm?

OPTIONS:
A. They are identical
B. An algorithm is always object-oriented; a pattern never is
C. A pattern is just a faster algorithm
D. A pattern arranges classes/objects for a design problem; an algorithm specifies computational steps

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. A pattern arranges classes/objects for a design problem; an algorithm specifies computational steps

RATIONALE:
A pattern arranges classes/objects to solve a design problem; an algorithm specifies computational steps. They are not identical, and neither is OO-exclusive.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns, ch. 1

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: foundations-what-is-pattern:A5
Paired Question ID: foundations-what-is-pattern:B5
Competency ID: pattern-adoption-value
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: pilot-reviewed

QUESTION:
A team keeps re-solving the same object-arrangement problem slightly differently across modules. What would adopting a design pattern give them?

OPTIONS:
A. A shared, named solution shape they can reuse and recognize
B. Automatically fixed bugs
C. A smaller compiled binary
D. Guaranteed thread safety

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. A shared, named solution shape they can reuse and recognize

RATIONALE:
Adopting a pattern gives a shared, reusable, recognizable solution shape. It does not auto-fix bugs, shrink the binary, or guarantee thread safety.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns, ch. 1

Question 4

Competency ID: pattern-vs-other-concepts
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: pilot-reviewed

QUESTION:
Which statement correctly contrasts a pattern with a data structure?

OPTIONS:
A. They are the same thing
B. A data structure is always a pattern
C. A pattern arranges classes/responsibilities for a design problem; a data structure organizes data for access
D. A pattern can be used only once

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. A pattern arranges classes/responsibilities for a design problem; a data structure organizes data for access

RATIONALE:
A pattern arranges classes/responsibilities for a design problem; a data structure organizes data for access/storage. They are not the same, and a pattern is reusable.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns, ch. 1

MODULE: Why design patterns matter
MODULE ID: foundations-why-matters

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: foundations-why-matters:A1
Paired Question ID: foundations-why-matters:B1
Competency ID: why-patterns-matter
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the main practical benefit of a shared design-pattern vocabulary on a team?

OPTIONS:
A. It lets a recognised name stand in for a paragraph of structural explanation
B. It makes the compiled program run faster
C. It removes the need for any code comments
D. It guarantees the code has no bugs

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. It lets a recognised name stand in for a paragraph of structural explanation

RATIONALE:
Pattern names are a communication tool: one agreed term conveys a known structure and intent, shortening design discussion and review. They do not affect runtime speed or correctness.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns, ch. 1

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: foundations-why-matters:A2
Paired Question ID: foundations-why-matters:B2
Competency ID: why-patterns-matter
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Why does recognising a pattern in existing code help a reviewer?

OPTIONS:
A. It proves the code is the fastest possible implementation
B. It lets the reviewer infer intent and expected structure quickly, focusing review on real risks
C. It means the code needs no tests
D. It shows the author used the newest language features

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. It lets the reviewer infer intent and expected structure quickly, focusing review on real risks

RATIONALE:
Recognising intent lets the reviewer reason about responsibilities and collaborations instead of re-deriving them, so attention goes to genuine risks. It says nothing about speed, tests, or language features.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: foundations-why-matters:A3
Paired Question ID: foundations-why-matters:B3
Competency ID: why-patterns-matter
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Which statement best explains a limitation of patterns?

OPTIONS:
A. A pattern guarantees the design is correct for every problem
B. Patterns can only be used in object-oriented languages
C. Applying a pattern where it is not needed can add complexity without benefit
D. Patterns make code impossible to refactor later

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Applying a pattern where it is not needed can add complexity without benefit

RATIONALE:
Patterns are solutions to specific recurring problems; forcing one where the problem is absent adds indirection and complexity for no gain. They are not universally correct, are not OO-exclusive, and do not block refactoring.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns, ch. 1

------------------------------------------------

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: foundations-why-matters:B1
Paired Question ID: foundations-why-matters:A1
Competency ID: why-patterns-matter
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Two engineers say “let’s use the Observer here.” What has the pattern name primarily given them?

OPTIONS:
A. A faster program at runtime
B. A guarantee the feature is bug-free
C. A way to skip writing the class entirely
D. A shared, compact way to communicate a known structure and intent

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. A shared, compact way to communicate a known structure and intent

RATIONALE:
The value is communication: the name conveys an agreed structure/intent so the team need not spell it out. It does not change runtime behaviour, remove bugs, or avoid implementation.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns, ch. 1

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: foundations-why-matters:B2
Paired Question ID: foundations-why-matters:A2
Competency ID: why-patterns-matter
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A new hire reads a module and recognises a familiar pattern. How does that most help them?

OPTIONS:
A. They can predict the responsibilities and collaborations without re-reading every line
B. They no longer need to run the test suite
C. They can be sure it is the most efficient code possible
D. They know it uses the latest standard-library features

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. They can predict the responsibilities and collaborations without re-reading every line

RATIONALE:
Recognition transfers prior knowledge of the pattern’s roles and interactions, speeding comprehension. It does not imply optimal performance, remove the need for tests, or indicate language features.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: foundations-why-matters:B3
Paired Question ID: foundations-why-matters:A3
Competency ID: why-patterns-matter
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
When can adopting a design pattern be the wrong choice?

OPTIONS:
A. When the team already understands the pattern
B. When the underlying recurring problem the pattern solves is not actually present
C. Whenever the language supports inheritance
D. When the code must be unit tested

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. When the underlying recurring problem the pattern solves is not actually present

RATIONALE:
A pattern earns its complexity only when its target problem exists; applying it speculatively adds indirection without benefit. Team familiarity, inheritance support, and testing are not reasons to avoid it.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Foundations module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns, ch. 1

CATEGORY: CREATIONAL
MODULE: Abstract Factory
MODULE ID: creational-abstract-factory
ASSESSMENT TYPE: Formal Pre-Test — Form A
APPLICABLE BLOOM LEVELS: Remember, Understand, Apply, Analyze, Evaluate
NON-APPLICABLE LEVELS: (none)
================================================================Question 1
Question ID: creational-abstract-factory:A1
Paired Question ID: creational-abstract-factory:B1
Competency ID: af-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Abstract Factory pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Provide an interface for creating families of related objects without naming their concrete classes
B. Ensure a single instance
C. Copy an existing object
D. Add behaviour to an object dynamically

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Provide an interface for creating families of related objects without naming their concrete classes

RATIONALE:
Abstract Factory creates families of related products behind one interface, hiding concrete classes. The distractors are Singleton, Prototype, and Decorator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Creational module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: creational-abstract-factory:A2
Paired Question ID: creational-abstract-factory:B2
Competency ID: af-understand
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How does Abstract Factory differ from Factory Method?

OPTIONS:
A. It is identical to Factory Method
B. It creates a single product, while Factory Method creates families
C. It creates whole families of related products, while Factory Method creates one product via subclass override
D. It only applies to Singletons

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. It creates whole families of related products, while Factory Method creates one product via subclass override

RATIONALE:
Abstract Factory builds families of related products together; Factory Method defers one product’s creation to a subclass. They are not identical.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: creational-abstract-factory:A3
Paired Question ID: creational-abstract-factory:B3
Competency ID: af-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A UI toolkit must produce matching widgets (button, checkbox, menu) per theme, guaranteed to belong to the same look-and-feel. Which pattern fits?

OPTIONS:
A. Prototype
B. Singleton
C. Method Chaining
D. Abstract Factory

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Abstract Factory

RATIONALE:
Producing a coherent family of related widgets per theme is Abstract Factory; the distractors do not enforce matching families.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: creational-abstract-factory:A4
Paired Question ID: creational-abstract-factory:B4
Competency ID: af-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  struct GuiFactory { virtual Button* makeButton() = 0; virtual Checkbox* makeCheckbox() = 0; };  Which pattern is this interface part of?

OPTIONS:
A. Factory Method
B. Builder
C. Abstract Factory
D. Prototype

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Abstract Factory

RATIONALE:
One factory interface with multiple create methods for related products (Button + Checkbox) is Abstract Factory; a single create method would be Factory Method.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: creational-abstract-factory:A5
Paired Question ID: creational-abstract-factory:B5
Competency ID: af-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A concrete WinFactory implements makeButton() returning a WinButton but makeCheckbox() returns a MacCheckbox. Does this honor Abstract Factory’s intent?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — any concrete returns are fine
B. Yes — provided both compile
C. No — Abstract Factory forbids more than one product
D. No — a concrete factory must return products from one consistent family; mixing Win and Mac breaks the family guarantee

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. No — a concrete factory must return products from one consistent family; mixing Win and Mac breaks the family guarantee

RATIONALE:
Abstract Factory’s value is that one concrete factory yields a matching family; returning a Mac checkbox from a Windows factory violates that guarantee.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Abstract Factory is best described as a pattern that ___.

A. guarantees one global instance

D. assembles one object via ordered steps

Question 2
Question ID: creational-abstract-factory:B2
Paired Question ID: creational-abstract-factory:A2
Competency ID: af-understand
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Which statement correctly contrasts Abstract Factory with Factory Method?

OPTIONS:
A. Abstract Factory makes one product; Factory Method makes families
B. They are the same pattern
C. Abstract Factory only works with Singletons
D. Abstract Factory creates a family of related products; Factory Method creates a single product via an overridden method

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Abstract Factory creates a family of related products; Factory Method creates a single product via an overridden method

RATIONALE:
Abstract Factory creates coherent families; Factory Method creates one product through a subclass override. The other options invert or conflate them.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

OPTIONS:
A. Abstract Factory
B. Prototype
C. Singleton
D. Adapter

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Abstract Factory

RATIONALE:
Creating a coherent, non-mixed family per platform is Abstract Factory; the distractors do not enforce family coherence.

* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

QUESTION:
Examine:  struct WinFactory : GuiFactory { Button* makeButton() override { return new WinButton(); } Checkbox* makeCheckbox() override { return new WinCheckbox(); } };  Which pattern does this concrete class participate in?

OPTIONS:
A. Builder
B. Prototype
C. Singleton
D. Abstract Factory

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Abstract Factory

RATIONALE:
A concrete factory implementing several related create methods to return one consistent family (WinButton + WinCheckbox) is Abstract Factory.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

Question 5
Question ID: creational-abstract-factory:B5
Paired Question ID: creational-abstract-factory:A5
Competency ID: af-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A team replaces two separate makeButton()/makeCheckbox() factory methods with one makeWidget(string kind) switch returning new-ed widgets. Is this still Abstract Factory?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — it is unchanged
B. No — collapsing related product methods into a string switch loses the typed family interface that defines Abstract Factory
C. Yes — provided the class is named Factory
D. No — Abstract Factory requires Singletons

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. No — collapsing related product methods into a string switch loses the typed family interface that defines Abstract Factory

RATIONALE:
Abstract Factory relies on a typed interface of related create methods producing a coherent family; a single stringly-typed switch discards that structure.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: creational-builder:A1
Paired Question ID: creational-builder:B1
Competency ID: builder-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: pilot-reviewed

QUESTION:
What is the primary intent of the Builder pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Separate the construction of a complex object from its representation, so the same process can create different representations
B. Ensure a class has only one instance
C. Convert one interface into another
D. Notify dependents when state changes

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Separate the construction of a complex object from its representation, so the same process can create different representations

RATIONALE:
Builder separates constructing a complex object from its representation so one process can yield different representations. The distractors describe Singleton, Adapter, and Observer.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma et al. (1994), Design Patterns — Builder
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++ — Builder

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: creational-builder:A2
Paired Question ID: creational-builder:B2
Competency ID: builder-construction-organization
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: pilot-reviewed

QUESTION:
How does the Builder pattern typically organize object construction?

OPTIONS:
A. By making all fields public and setting them directly
B. By using a single giant constructor with many parameters
C. Through a sequence of step methods that assemble parts, then a final build step
D. By cloning an existing instance

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Through a sequence of step methods that assemble parts, then a final build step

RATIONALE:
Builder assembles parts via step methods then a final build step, avoiding public mutable fields, telescoping constructors, and cloning.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma et al. (1994), Design Patterns — Builder
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++ — Builder

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: creational-builder:A3
Paired Question ID: creational-builder:B3
Competency ID: builder-select-for-requirement
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: pilot-reviewed

QUESTION:
A system must assemble configuration objects through a series of optional, ordered steps and produce several different final representations from the same steps. Which pattern best fits?

OPTIONS:
A. Singleton
B. Builder
C. Adapter
D. Observer

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Builder

RATIONALE:
Optional, ordered steps producing different final representations is Builder’s core use case; Singleton, Adapter, and Observer solve unrelated problems.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma et al. (1994), Design Patterns — Builder
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++ — Builder

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: creational-builder:A4
Paired Question ID: creational-builder:B4
Competency ID: builder-identify-from-structure
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: pilot-reviewed

QUESTION:
A class exposes addPart()/setX() methods that each return the same object, plus a final build() that returns the assembled product; no part is exposed until build(). Which pattern does this structure indicate?

OPTIONS:
A. Prototype
B. Facade
C. Composite
D. Builder

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Builder

RATIONALE:
Chained part-adding methods returning the same object plus a terminal build() that first exposes the product is Builder’s structural signature.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma et al. (1994), Design Patterns — Builder
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++ — Builder

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: creational-builder:A5
Paired Question ID: creational-builder:B5
Competency ID: builder-evaluate-tradeoff
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: pilot-reviewed

QUESTION:
An object has many optional parameters and must remain immutable after creation. Which is more appropriate, and why — a step-based assembler that returns a finished product, or a telescoping set of constructors?

OPTIONS:
A. The step-based assembler — it handles optional parameters cleanly and yields an immutable result
B. Telescoping constructors — they are always clearer
C. Neither; expose public setters instead
D. A single global instance

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. The step-based assembler — it handles optional parameters cleanly and yields an immutable result

RATIONALE:
For many optional parameters plus immutability, a step assembler returning a finished product is clearest and preserves immutability; telescoping constructors and setters do not.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma et al. (1994), Design Patterns — Builder
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++ — Builder

The Builder pattern is mainly used to ___.

Question 2

Paired Question ID: creational-builder:A2
Competency ID: builder-construction-organization
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: pilot-reviewed

QUESTION:
Why does Builder separate a step sequence from the part-assembly?

OPTIONS:
A. To make the object a singleton
B. To avoid writing any classes
C. To convert interfaces
D. So the same construction sequence can yield different representations while construction details stay encapsulated

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. So the same construction sequence can yield different representations while construction details stay encapsulated

RATIONALE:
Separating the step sequence from assembly lets one sequence yield different representations while hiding construction details — Builder’s rationale.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma et al. (1994), Design Patterns — Builder
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++ — Builder

B. Proxy

C. Builder

QUESTION:
You see a fluent class whose methods return *this to chain calls and a terminal build() that produces the final object only at the end. Which pattern is this structural evidence of?

OPTIONS:
A. Builder
B. Decorator
C. State
D. Mediator

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Builder

RATIONALE:
A fluent class returning *this to chain calls with a terminal build() is structural evidence of Builder; the distractors have different shapes.

Question 5
Question ID: creational-builder:B5
Paired Question ID: creational-builder:A5
Competency ID: builder-evaluate-tradeoff
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: pilot-reviewed

QUESTION:
A complex object needs many optional fields and must be validated once, fully assembled. Which approach better meets "assemble incrementally, validate on completion" — a step assembler with a final build, or setting fields directly after construction?

OPTIONS:
A. Direct field setting after construction
B. A step assembler with a final build/validate step
C. A global mutable instance
D. Cloning a prototype each time

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. A step assembler with a final build/validate step

RATIONALE:
Incremental assembly with validation on completion is met by a step assembler with a final build/validate step; direct setters, globals, and cloning do not.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma et al. (1994), Design Patterns — Builder
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++ — Builder

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: creational-factory-method:A1
Paired Question ID: creational-factory-method:B1
Competency ID: fm-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Factory Method pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Define an interface for creating an object but let subclasses decide which concrete class to instantiate
B. Ensure a class has a single instance
C. Copy an existing object
D. Wrap an incompatible interface

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Define an interface for creating an object but let subclasses decide which concrete class to instantiate

RATIONALE:
Factory Method defers the choice of concrete product to subclasses via an overridable creation method. The distractors describe Singleton, Prototype, and Adapter.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Creational module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: creational-factory-method:A2
Paired Question ID: creational-factory-method:B2
Competency ID: fm-understand
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
In Factory Method, who decides which concrete product is created?

OPTIONS:
A. The client’s calling code directly
B. The compiler
C. A subclass that overrides the creation method
D. A global registry only

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. A subclass that overrides the creation method

RATIONALE:
The creator declares a creation method; a subclass overrides it to choose the concrete product, decoupling clients from concretes.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: creational-factory-method:A3
Paired Question ID: creational-factory-method:B3
Competency ID: fm-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A framework defines a workflow but must let each plug-in decide which concrete document object the workflow creates. Which pattern fits?

OPTIONS:
A. Singleton
B. Prototype
C. Method Chaining
D. Factory Method

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Factory Method

RATIONALE:
Letting subclasses/plug-ins choose the concrete product while the base defines the workflow is Factory Method; the distractors solve other problems.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 4
Question ID: creational-factory-method:A4
Paired Question ID: creational-factory-method:B4
Competency ID: fm-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  struct Creator { virtual Product* create() = 0; void run(){ auto* p = create(); /* use p */ } };  struct PdfCreator : Creator { Product* create() override { return new Pdf(); } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Factory Method
B. Abstract Factory
C. Builder
D. Singleton

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Factory Method

RATIONALE:
A base class with an overridable create() that subclasses implement to pick the concrete product is Factory Method (one product per call), not a family factory or step builder.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

Question 5
Question ID: creational-factory-method:A5
Paired Question ID: creational-factory-method:B5
Competency ID: fm-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A class named ShapeFactory contains one giant switch on a string to new each shape, with no polymorphic creator. Is this Factory Method?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — the name ShapeFactory makes it Factory Method
B. Yes — any creation code is Factory Method
C. No — Factory Method requires templates
D. No — the name does not matter; without a polymorphic creation method overridden by subclasses it is a simple/switch factory, not Factory Method

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. No — the name does not matter; without a polymorphic creation method overridden by subclasses it is a simple/switch factory, not Factory Method

RATIONALE:
Factory Method’s defining structure is a creation method overridden by subclasses; a string switch is a simple factory. The class name is not evidence of the pattern.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

QUESTION:
Factory Method is best described as a pattern that ___.

OPTIONS:
A. guarantees a single shared instance
B. produces whole families of related products
C. lets subclasses decide which concrete class an overridable method instantiates
D. adds behaviour around an object transparently

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. lets subclasses decide which concrete class an overridable method instantiates

RATIONALE:
Factory Method delegates the concrete-product choice to subclasses through an overridable method. The distractors are Singleton, Abstract Factory, and Decorator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Creational module content

Question 2
Question ID: creational-factory-method:B2
Paired Question ID: creational-factory-method:A2
Competency ID: fm-understand
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How does Factory Method decouple a creator from the concrete products it uses?

OPTIONS:
A. By making the product a global variable
B. By hard-coding the product type in the base class
C. By cloning a prototype instance
D. By calling an overridable creation method, so subclasses supply the concrete type

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. By calling an overridable creation method, so subclasses supply the concrete type

RATIONALE:
The creator calls its own overridable creation method; subclasses decide the concrete type, so the creator never names a concrete product.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

OPTIONS:
A. Singleton
B. Factory Method
C. Adapter
D. Prototype

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Factory Method

RATIONALE:
A shared flow whose concrete sub-object is chosen by subclasses is Factory Method; the distractors address single-instance, interface conversion, and copying.

* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

QUESTION:

OPTIONS:
A. Factory Method
B. Builder
C. Prototype
D. Abstract Factory

A. Factory Method

A virtual make() overridden by a subclass to return one concrete product is Factory Method; it produces a single product, not a family or a step-built object.

MODULE ID: creational-method-chaining
ASSESSMENT TYPE: Formal Pre-Test — Form A
APPLICABLE BLOOM LEVELS: Remember, Understand, Apply, Analyze, Evaluate
NON-APPLICABLE LEVELS: (none)
================================================================Question 1
Question ID: creational-method-chaining:A1
Paired Question ID: creational-method-chaining:B1
Competency ID: mc-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What does the Method Chaining idiom let you do?

OPTIONS:
A. Set several options on the same object in one fluent expression
B. Guarantee one instance
C. Create matching product families
D. Convert an incompatible interface

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Set several options on the same object in one fluent expression

RATIONALE:
Method Chaining returns the same object from each setter so calls can be chained fluently. The distractors are Singleton, Abstract Factory, and Adapter.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++
* CodiNeo Creational module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: creational-method-chaining:A2
Paired Question ID: creational-method-chaining:B2
Competency ID: mc-understand
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What must a setter return to enable method chaining in C++?

OPTIONS:
A. void
B. a new copy of the object
C. a reference (or pointer) to the same object, e.g. return *this
D. a boolean success flag

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. a reference (or pointer) to the same object, e.g. return *this

RATIONALE:
Returning *this (a reference to the same object) lets the next call chain on it. Returning void, a copy, or a flag breaks the chain.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: creational-method-chaining:A3
Paired Question ID: creational-method-chaining:B3
Competency ID: mc-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A configuration object should be set up in one readable expression like cfg.host("x").port(80).tls(true);. Which technique enables this?

OPTIONS:
A. Returning void from each setter
B. Making the class a Singleton
C. Cloning the object per setter
D. Having each setter return a reference to the object so calls chain

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Having each setter return a reference to the object so calls chain

RATIONALE:
Each setter returning a self-reference makes the fluent chain possible. The other options prevent chaining or solve unrelated problems.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: creational-method-chaining:A4
Paired Question ID: creational-method-chaining:B4
Competency ID: mc-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  struct Pizza { Pizza& size(int s){ s_=s; return *this; } Pizza& cheese(bool c){ c_=c; return *this; } };  What idiom does this show?

OPTIONS:
A. Method Chaining (fluent interface)
B. Prototype
C. Singleton
D. Abstract Factory

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Method Chaining (fluent interface)

RATIONALE:
Setters that mutate and return *this form a fluent interface (Method Chaining). No copying, single-instance, or family creation is present.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: creational-method-chaining:A5
Paired Question ID: creational-method-chaining:B5
Competency ID: mc-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A “fluent” API returns a brand-new copy of the object from every setter instead of *this. Does this correctly implement method chaining, and what is the effect?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — it is identical to returning *this
B. Yes — copies are required for chaining
C. No — chaining is impossible in C++
D. No — returning copies means earlier settings may be lost and each call mutates a different object, defeating the fluent intent

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. No — returning copies means earlier settings may be lost and each call mutates a different object, defeating the fluent intent

RATIONALE:
Returning fresh copies means each call operates on a different object, so accumulated configuration can be lost; chaining requires returning the same object (*this).

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++
