Question 2
Question ID: creational-method-chaining:B2
Paired Question ID: creational-method-chaining:A2
Competency ID: mc-understand
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Why does each chained method return *this?

OPTIONS:
A. To make a defensive copy
B. To enforce a single instance
C. To convert the return type
D. So the following call can operate on the same object, continuing the chain

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. So the following call can operate on the same object, continuing the chain

RATIONALE:
Returning *this hands the same object to the next call, sustaining the fluent chain. The other options are unrelated.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: creational-method-chaining:B3
Paired Question ID: creational-method-chaining:A3
Competency ID: mc-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A query builder should read like q.select("*").from("t").where("id=1");. What enables this fluent style?

OPTIONS:
A. Each method returns void
B. Each method returns a reference to the builder so calls chain
C. The builder is cloned each call
D. The builder is a Singleton

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Each method returns a reference to the builder so calls chain

RATIONALE:
Each method returning a reference to the builder enables chaining; void, cloning, or Singleton would not.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: creational-method-chaining:B4
Paired Question ID: creational-method-chaining:A4
Competency ID: mc-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  Query& Query::where(std::string c){ clauses_.push_back(c); return *this; }  // similar for select(), from()  Which idiom is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Method Chaining (fluent interface)
B. Singleton
C. Prototype
D. Factory Method

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Method Chaining (fluent interface)

RATIONALE:
Mutating member functions that return *this form a fluent (method-chaining) interface; there is no single-instance, copy, or factory structure.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: creational-method-chaining:B5
Paired Question ID: creational-method-chaining:A5
Competency ID: mc-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A setter in a “fluent” class returns void but the author still writes a.x().y();. Is the chaining implemented correctly?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — void return is fine for chaining
B. Yes — provided the methods are const
C. No — with a void return the second call has no object to act on, so it will not compile/chain
D. No — only Singletons can chain

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. No — with a void return the second call has no object to act on, so it will not compile/chain

RATIONALE:
Chaining needs each call to yield the object; a void return leaves nothing to call the next method on, so the chain cannot work.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

================================================================Question 1

Paired Question ID: creational-prototype:B1
Competency ID: pr-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Prototype pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Create new objects by copying an existing instance (a prototype)
B. Guarantee a single instance
C. Let subclasses choose the concrete product
D. Assemble an object through ordered steps

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Create new objects by copying an existing instance (a prototype)

RATIONALE:
Prototype creates new objects by cloning an existing prototypical instance. The distractors are Singleton, Factory Method, and Builder.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Creational module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: creational-prototype:A2
Paired Question ID: creational-prototype:B2
Competency ID: pr-understand
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Why is Prototype useful when object creation is expensive or configuration-heavy?

OPTIONS:
A. It avoids creating objects at all
B. It copies a ready, pre-configured instance instead of rebuilding from scratch
C. It guarantees thread safety
D. It removes the need for constructors

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. It copies a ready, pre-configured instance instead of rebuilding from scratch

RATIONALE:
Cloning a pre-configured prototype reuses its setup, avoiding costly re-construction. The other options misstate Prototype’s benefit.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: creational-prototype:A3
Paired Question ID: creational-prototype:B3
Competency ID: pr-select

Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
An editor must duplicate a fully-configured shape (with styling) at runtime without knowing its concrete type. Which pattern fits?

OPTIONS:
A. Singleton
B. Abstract Factory
C. Method Chaining
D. Prototype

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Prototype

RATIONALE:
Duplicating a configured object polymorphically (without its concrete type) is Prototype via clone(); the distractors solve other problems.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 4
Question ID: creational-prototype:A4
Paired Question ID: creational-prototype:B4
Competency ID: pr-identify

Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  struct Shape { virtual Shape* clone() const = 0; };  struct Circle : Shape { Shape* clone() const override { return new Circle(*this); } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Prototype
B. Factory Method
C. Builder
D. Singleton

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Prototype

RATIONALE:
A virtual clone() that copy-constructs the same concrete type is the Prototype structure; it copies rather than choosing or assembling a product.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: creational-prototype:A5
Paired Question ID: creational-prototype:B5
Competency ID: pr-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A clone() returns new Base(*this) for every subclass (no override), so cloning a Derived yields a Base. Is this a correct Prototype, and why?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — base copying is sufficient
B. Yes — provided Base is abstract
C. No — Prototype forbids inheritance
D. No — without a polymorphic override clone() slices the object, losing the derived type

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. No — without a polymorphic override clone() slices the object, losing the derived type

RATIONALE:
Cloning must be polymorphic so each subclass copies its own type; a fixed new Base(*this) slices derived objects, breaking Prototype.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

QUESTION:
The Prototype pattern creates new objects by ___.

OPTIONS:
A. assembling them step by step
B. sharing a single global instance
C. cloning an existing pre-built instance
D. producing related families

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. cloning an existing pre-built instance

RATIONALE:
Prototype clones an existing instance to make new ones. The distractors are Builder, Singleton, and Abstract Factory.

* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 2

Paired Question ID: creational-prototype:A2
Competency ID: pr-understand
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What advantage does cloning a prototype give over constructing from scratch?

OPTIONS:
A. It eliminates memory allocation
B. It reuses an already-configured instance, avoiding repeated setup
C. It guarantees a single instance
D. It converts interfaces

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. It reuses an already-configured instance, avoiding repeated setup

RATIONALE:
Cloning carries over the prototype’s configuration, saving repeated setup work; the other options are not Prototype benefits.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

QUESTION:
A game spawns many enemies identical to a configured template at runtime, varying only position. Which pattern most directly applies?

OPTIONS:
A. Prototype
B. Singleton
C. Factory Method
D. Adapter

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Prototype

RATIONALE:
Spawning copies of a configured template is Prototype (clone then tweak); the distractors do not copy a configured instance.

* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 4
Question ID: creational-prototype:B4
Paired Question ID: creational-prototype:A4
Competency ID: pr-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  Document* dup = original->clone();  where clone() is virtual and each subclass returns a copy of itself. Which pattern is this evidence of?

OPTIONS:
A. Builder
B. Abstract Factory
C. Prototype
D. Singleton

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Prototype

RATIONALE:
A polymorphic clone() that returns a copy of the concrete subclass is the Prototype pattern; no stepwise build or family creation is involved.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

Question 5
Question ID: creational-prototype:B5
Paired Question ID: creational-prototype:A5
Competency ID: pr-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A Prototype holds a raw pointer member and clone() does a shallow copy. What is the risk, and is it a sound clone?

OPTIONS:
A. No risk — shallow copy is always correct
B. Sound only if the class is final
C. Unsound — Prototype cannot hold pointers
D. Risky/unsound — both objects share the same pointed-to resource, so a deep copy is usually needed for an independent clone

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Risky/unsound — both objects share the same pointed-to resource, so a deep copy is usually needed for an independent clone

RATIONALE:
A shallow copy makes clones share the same owned resource, causing aliasing/double-free bugs; an independent clone usually needs a deep copy.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: creational-singleton:A1
Paired Question ID: creational-singleton:B1
Competency ID: singleton-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What does the Singleton pattern guarantee?

OPTIONS:
A. A class has exactly one instance with a single global access point
B. A class can be freely subclassed and copied
C. Objects are produced in matching families
D. Objects are built through ordered steps

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. A class has exactly one instance with a single global access point

RATIONALE:
Singleton ensures a class has exactly one instance and provides one global access point to it. The other options describe subclassing, Abstract Factory, and Builder.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Creational module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: creational-singleton:A2
Paired Question ID: creational-singleton:B2
Competency ID: singleton-enforce
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How is the single-instance guarantee typically enforced in C++?

OPTIONS:
A. By marking every method const
B. By making the class abstract
C. By a non-public constructor plus a static accessor that returns the sole instance
D. By deleting the destructor

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. By a non-public constructor plus a static accessor that returns the sole instance

RATIONALE:
A hidden (non-public) constructor prevents outside construction, and a static accessor returns the one instance. The other options do not constrain instance count.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++
* CodiNeo Creational module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: creational-singleton:A3
Paired Question ID: creational-singleton:B3
Competency ID: singleton-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A system needs exactly one shared configuration object, created once and reachable everywhere. Which pattern fits?

OPTIONS:
A. Prototype
B. Factory Method
C. Abstract Factory
D. Singleton

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Singleton

RATIONALE:
One shared instance with a global access point is the Singleton use case; the distractors address copying, deferred subclass choice, and product families.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: creational-singleton:A4
Paired Question ID: creational-singleton:B4
Competency ID: singleton-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Cfg { Cfg() = default; public: static Cfg& instance() { static Cfg c; return c; } };  Which pattern does this indicate?

OPTIONS:
A. Singleton
B. Factory Method
C. Builder
D. Prototype

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Singleton

RATIONALE:
A non-public constructor plus a static accessor returning one function-local static is the (Meyers) Singleton idiom; nothing here creates families, products, or copies.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: creational-singleton:A5
Paired Question ID: creational-singleton:B5
Competency ID: singleton-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A class exposes a public static getInstance() but also leaves its constructor public. Does this correctly implement Singleton, and why?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — the static getter is sufficient
B. Yes — provided the class is marked final
C. No — Singletons must allocate on the heap
D. No — a public constructor lets callers create extra instances, breaking the single-instance guarantee

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. No — a public constructor lets callers create extra instances, breaking the single-instance guarantee

RATIONALE:
The single-instance invariant requires preventing outside construction; a public constructor allows additional instances, so the implementation is incorrect regardless of the getter.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate

QUESTION:
Which member combination is characteristic of a C++ Singleton?

OPTIONS:
A. A public constructor and a virtual destructor
B. Several overloaded public constructors
C. A public clone() method
D. A hidden constructor and a static method returning the sole instance

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. A hidden constructor and a static method returning the sole instance

RATIONALE:
A hidden constructor blocks external construction while a static accessor returns the one instance. Public/overloaded constructors permit many instances; clone() is Prototype.

* CodiNeo Creational module content

A. Builder
B. Singleton
C. Prototype
D. Adapter

B. Singleton

RATIONALE:
A single shared, lazily-created object with global access is Singleton; the distractors solve construction, copying, and interface conversion.

QUESTION:

OPTIONS:
A. Singleton
B. Prototype
C. Abstract Factory
D. Method Chaining

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Singleton

RATIONALE:
A private constructor plus a function-local static returned by reference is the lazy (Meyers) Singleton; it is not copying, family creation, or fluent chaining.

Question 5

QUESTION:
A developer keeps the instance in a global pointer and calls new in several places “just in case.” Is this a sound Singleton?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — a global pointer guarantees uniqueness
B. Yes — provided it is wrapped in a namespace
C. No — multiple new calls create multiple instances, violating the single-instance invariant
D. No — Singletons cannot use pointers at all

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. No — multiple new calls create multiple instances, violating the single-instance invariant

RATIONALE:
Calling new in several places creates several instances, breaking uniqueness; the access mechanism (global pointer/namespace) does not enforce a single instance.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: structural-adapter:A1
Paired Question ID: structural-adapter:B1
Competency ID: ad-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Adapter pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Convert a class’s interface into another interface clients expect, so incompatible types can work together
B. Add new behaviour to an object by wrapping it
C. Control or restrict access to an object
D. Ensure a class has only one instance

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Convert a class’s interface into another interface clients expect, so incompatible types can work together

RATIONALE:
Adapter converts one existing interface into the interface a client expects. The distractors describe Decorator, Proxy, and Singleton.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: structural-adapter:A2
Paired Question ID: structural-adapter:B2
Competency ID: ad-mechanism
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How does an Adapter make two incompatible interfaces work together?

OPTIONS:
A. It edits the adaptee’s source code to match the client
B. It implements the target interface and translates each call to the adaptee’s existing methods
C. It duplicates the adaptee for each client
D. It deletes the target interface entirely

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. It implements the target interface and translates each call to the adaptee’s existing methods

RATIONALE:
The adapter conforms to the target interface and forwards/translates calls to the unchanged adaptee. It does not modify or copy the adaptee.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: structural-adapter:A3
Paired Question ID: structural-adapter:B3
Competency ID: ad-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A new reporting client expects a JSON-exporter interface, but the only library available exposes a completely different XML API you cannot change. Which pattern lets them work together?

OPTIONS:
A. Decorator
B. Proxy
C. Adapter
D. Composite

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Adapter

RATIONALE:
Bridging a required interface to an unchangeable, differently-shaped existing one is Adapter. The distractors add behaviour, control access, or build trees.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: structural-adapter:A4
Paired Question ID: structural-adapter:B4
Competency ID: ad-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class JsonTarget { public: virtual std::string toJson() = 0; };  class XmlLib { public: std::string toXml(); };  class XmlToJson : public JsonTarget { XmlLib lib; public: std::string toJson() override { return convert(lib.toXml()); } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Bridge
B. Facade
C. Proxy
D. Adapter

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Adapter

RATIONALE:
A class implementing the expected interface (JsonTarget) by translating to an existing, differently-shaped API (XmlLib) is Adapter.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: structural-adapter:A5
Paired Question ID: structural-adapter:B5
Competency ID: ad-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A class claims to be an Adapter but simply adds caching around the very same interface the client already used, converting nothing. Is this correctly an Adapter?

OPTIONS:
A. No — Adapter must convert one interface into another; adding behaviour over the same interface is Decorator/Proxy, not Adapter
B. Yes — any wrapper is an Adapter
C. Yes — caching counts as adapting
D. No — Adapters cannot use composition

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. No — Adapter must convert one interface into another; adding behaviour over the same interface is Decorator/Proxy, not Adapter

RATIONALE:
Adapter’s defining purpose is interface conversion. Adding behaviour while keeping the same interface is Decorator/Proxy intent, not Adapter.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

QUESTION:
The Adapter pattern is best described as a way to ___.

OPTIONS:
A. guarantee a single shared instance
B. make one existing interface usable through a different, expected interface
C. share intrinsic state across many objects
D. pass a request along a chain of handlers

CORRECT ANSWER:

Adapter makes an existing interface usable through the interface a client expects. The distractors are Singleton, Flyweight, and Chain of Responsibility.

Question 2
Question ID: structural-adapter:B2
Paired Question ID: structural-adapter:A2
Competency ID: ad-mechanism
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the adapter’s role relative to the adaptee and the client?

OPTIONS:
A. It changes the client to match the adaptee
B. It merges client and adaptee into one class
C. It conforms to the interface the client expects and delegates work to the adaptee
D. It hides the adaptee permanently from all code

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. It conforms to the interface the client expects and delegates work to the adaptee

RATIONALE:
The adapter presents the client-expected interface and delegates to the adaptee, leaving both client and adaptee unchanged.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 3
Question ID: structural-adapter:B3
Paired Question ID: structural-adapter:A3
Competency ID: ad-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
An app’s payment module calls charge(Money), but a third-party SDK you cannot modify only offers processPayment(double, std::string). Which pattern bridges them?

OPTIONS:
A. Composite
B. Facade
C. Decorator
D. Adapter

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Adapter

RATIONALE:
Reconciling a required call shape with an unchangeable foreign API is Adapter. The distractors build trees, simplify subsystems, or add behaviour.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 4
Question ID: structural-adapter:B4
Paired Question ID: structural-adapter:A4
Competency ID: ad-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Logger { public: virtual void log(std::string) = 0; };  class LegacyWriter { public: void writeLine(const char*); };  class LegacyLogAdapter : public Logger { LegacyWriter w; public: void log(std::string s) override { w.writeLine(s.c_str()); } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Adapter
B. Decorator
C. Proxy
D. Bridge

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Adapter

RATIONALE:
Implementing the expected Logger interface by translating to a legacy method with a different signature is Adapter.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Paired Question ID: structural-adapter:A5

Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A wrapper implements the expected RateProvider interface and internally calls an unrelated CurrencyApi with a different signature, translating the arguments. Is calling this an Adapter defensible?

OPTIONS:
A. No — Adapters cannot translate arguments
B. Yes — it implements the expected interface and converts calls to a different existing interface, which is exactly Adapter
C. No — this is always Facade
D. Yes — but only if it also adds new features

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Yes — it implements the expected interface and converts calls to a different existing interface, which is exactly Adapter

RATIONALE:
Implementing the expected interface while converting calls to a differently-shaped existing API is the definition of Adapter; argument translation is normal.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: structural-bridge:A1
Paired Question ID: structural-bridge:B1
Competency ID: br-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Bridge pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Add behaviour by wrapping
B. Provide a surrogate for access
C. Decouple an abstraction from its implementation so the two can vary independently
D. Compose objects into trees

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Decouple an abstraction from its implementation so the two can vary independently

RATIONALE:
Bridge separates an abstraction from its implementation so both vary independently. The distractors are Decorator, Proxy, and Composite.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: structural-bridge:A2
Paired Question ID: structural-bridge:B2
Competency ID: br-vs-inherit
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How does Bridge differ from plain inheritance for handling two dimensions of variation?

OPTIONS:
A. It uses one giant subclass per combination
B. It forbids polymorphism
C. It merges both dimensions into one class
D. It composes the abstraction with a separate implementor hierarchy, avoiding a combinatorial explosion of subclasses

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. It composes the abstraction with a separate implementor hierarchy, avoiding a combinatorial explosion of subclasses

RATIONALE:
Bridge composes (not inherits) abstraction with an independent implementor hierarchy, avoiding the N×M subclass explosion.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: structural-bridge:A3
Paired Question ID: structural-bridge:B3
Competency ID: br-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Shapes (circle, square) must render via multiple drawing backends (OpenGL, SVG), and both sets will keep growing independently. Which pattern avoids one subclass per shape×backend pair?

OPTIONS:
A. Bridge
B. Decorator
C. Adapter
D. Composite

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Bridge

RATIONALE:
Two independently-varying dimensions handled by composition is Bridge. The distractors add behaviour, convert, or build trees.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: structural-bridge:A4
Paired Question ID: structural-bridge:B4
Competency ID: br-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Renderer { public: virtual void drawCircle(float) = 0; };  class Shape { protected: Renderer* r; public: Shape(Renderer* rr) : r(rr) {} virtual void draw() = 0; };  class Circle : public Shape { float rad; public: void draw() override { r->drawCircle(rad); } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Strategy
B. Bridge
C. Proxy
D. Template Method

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Bridge

RATIONALE:
An abstraction hierarchy (Shape) holding and delegating to a separate implementor hierarchy (Renderer) that both vary independently is Bridge.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 5
Question ID: structural-bridge:A5
Paired Question ID: structural-bridge:B5
Competency ID: br-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A developer claims a class uses Bridge because it holds a pointer to an implementation object. But there is only one implementation, used solely to hide private members from the header. Is this Bridge?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — any pointer-to-impl is Bridge
B. Yes — Bridge and PIMPL are the same
C. No — that is the PIMPL idiom; Bridge requires independently varying abstraction and implementation hierarchies
D. No — Bridge cannot use pointers

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. No — that is the PIMPL idiom; Bridge requires independently varying abstraction and implementation hierarchies

RATIONALE:
A single fixed implementation pointer used only to hide members is the PIMPL idiom; Bridge needs genuine, independently varying abstraction and implementor hierarchies.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

QUESTION:
Bridge’s intent is to ___.

Question 2
Question ID: structural-bridge:B2
Paired Question ID: structural-bridge:A2
Competency ID: br-vs-inherit
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What distinguishes Bridge from the PIMPL idiom?

OPTIONS:
A. Bridge supports multiple interchangeable implementations of an abstraction, while PIMPL hides one implementation to stabilise a header
B. They are identical
C. PIMPL allows many implementations; Bridge allows only one
D. Bridge is a C++-only idiom

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Bridge supports multiple interchangeable implementations of an abstraction, while PIMPL hides one implementation to stabilise a header

RATIONALE:
Bridge’s implementor hierarchy genuinely varies; PIMPL has a single hidden implementation whose purpose is header insulation.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

B. Bridge

Two independently-growing dimensions (message kinds × channels) handled by composition is Bridge.

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Composite pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Convert interfaces between layers
B. Add behaviour by wrapping
C. Compose objects into part-whole trees and let clients treat individual objects and compositions uniformly
D. Provide a surrogate for access control

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Compose objects into part-whole trees and let clients treat individual objects and compositions uniformly

RATIONALE:
Composite builds part-whole trees treated uniformly. The distractors are Adapter, Decorator, and Proxy.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: structural-composite:A2
Paired Question ID: structural-composite:B2
Competency ID: cp-uniform
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What makes uniform treatment of leaves and composites possible?

OPTIONS:
A. Leaves inherit from composites
B. Composites store no children
C. A global registry maps node types
D. Both leaf and composite implement the same component interface

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Both leaf and composite implement the same component interface

RATIONALE:
A shared component interface lets clients call the same operations on a leaf or a whole subtree without distinguishing them.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------

Question ID: structural-composite:A3

Difficulty: Moderate

QUESTION:
A drawing app must treat a single shape and a group of shapes identically for operations like move() and draw(), with groups nesting arbitrarily. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Composite
B. Decorator
C. Proxy
D. Strategy

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Composite

RATIONALE:
Uniform treatment of single items and nested groups is Composite. The distractors add behaviour, control access, or swap algorithms.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 4
Question ID: structural-composite:A4
Paired Question ID: structural-composite:B4
Competency ID: cp-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Node { public: virtual int size() = 0; };  class File : public Node { int size() override { return sz; } };  class Folder : public Node { std::vector<Node*> kids; public: int size() override { int t = 0; for (auto* k : kids) t += k->size(); return t; } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Visitor
B. Composite
C. Decorator
D. Iterator

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Composite

RATIONALE:
A shared Node interface where a container recursively aggregates its children is the Composite part-whole structure.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 5
Question ID: structural-composite:A5
Paired Question ID: structural-composite:B5
Competency ID: cp-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A “Composite” design gives Folder a children list but File a totally different interface with unrelated method names, forcing clients to type-check each node. Does this satisfy Composite’s intent?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — as long as there is a tree
B. Yes — composites and leaves should differ
C. No — Composite requires a shared component interface so leaf and composite are treated uniformly; type-checking defeats it
D. No — Composite forbids storing children

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. No — Composite requires a shared component interface so leaf and composite are treated uniformly; type-checking defeats it

RATIONALE:
Uniform treatment via a common interface is the whole point; if clients must branch on node type, the pattern’s benefit is lost.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

QUESTION:
In Composite, how does a composite node usually implement an operation?

OPTIONS:
A. It delegates the operation to each of its children and combines the results
B. It ignores its children
C. It converts the child interface
D. It keeps only one child

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. It delegates the operation to each of its children and combines the results

RATIONALE:
A composite forwards the operation to each child and aggregates, which is what makes recursive trees work uniformly.

* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

QUESTION:
What is the key structural feature of Composite?

OPTIONS:
A. A component interface that both leaves and containers implement; containers hold a collection of components.

Question 2
Question ID: structural-composite:understanding
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 1
Bloom Level: Understanding
Item Type: mcq

QUESTION:
What does Composite do?

OPTIONS:
A. Composes objects into tree structures and lets clients treat individual objects and compositions uniformly.
B. Restricts a class to one instance.
C. Converts one interface to another.
D. Selects an algorithm at runtime.

CORRECT ANSWER: A. Composes objects into tree structures and lets clients treat individual objects and compositions uniformly.

CATEGORY: STRUCTURAL
MODULE: Decorator
MODULE ID: structural-decorator
ASSESSMENT TYPE: Formal Pre-Test — Form A
APPLICABLE BLOOM LEVELS: Remember, Understand, Apply, Analyze, Evaluate
NON-APPLICABLE LEVELS: (none)
================================================================Question 1
Question ID: structural-decorator:A1
Paired Question ID: structural-decorator:B1
Competency ID: dc-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Decorator pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Attach additional responsibilities to an object dynamically by wrapping it
B. Convert one interface into another
C. Ensure a class has one instance
D. Centralise communication between objects

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Attach additional responsibilities to an object dynamically by wrapping it

RATIONALE:
Decorator adds responsibilities to an object dynamically through wrapping. The distractors are Adapter, Singleton, and Mediator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: structural-decorator:A2
Paired Question ID: structural-decorator:B2
Competency ID: dc-stack
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Why can decorators be stacked on top of one another?

OPTIONS:
A. Because they edit the base class
B. Each decorator implements the same component interface and holds a component, so they nest
C. Because they share one global instance
D. Because they convert interfaces between layers

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Each decorator implements the same component interface and holds a component, so they nest

RATIONALE:
Each decorator is itself a component and wraps a component, so wrappers nest indefinitely while staying interchangeable with the base.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: structural-decorator:A3
Paired Question ID: structural-decorator:B3
Competency ID: dc-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
You must add optional, combinable features (encryption, compression, buffering) to a data stream at runtime without a subclass explosion, keeping the same stream interface. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Proxy
B. Adapter
C. Decorator
D. Facade

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Decorator

RATIONALE:
Dynamically composable responsibilities over a shared interface is Decorator. The distractors control access, convert, or simplify subsystems.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: structural-decorator:A4
Paired Question ID: structural-decorator:B4
Competency ID: dc-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Coffee { public: virtual int cost() = 0; };  class Basic : public Coffee { int cost() override { return 2; } };  class MilkDecorator : public Coffee { Coffee* inner; public: MilkDecorator(Coffee* c) : inner(c) {} int cost() override { return inner->cost() + 1; } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Composite
B. Adapter
C. Proxy
D. Decorator

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Decorator

RATIONALE:
A same-interface wrapper that augments the wrapped object’s result (adds cost) is Decorator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: structural-decorator:A5
Paired Question ID: structural-decorator:B5
Competency ID: dc-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A wrapper implements the same interface and adds a visible new feature (drawing a border) to what the client sees, delegating the rest. Best classified as?

OPTIONS:
A. Decorator — it adds new client-facing behaviour while preserving the interface
B. Proxy — any wrapper controls access
C. Adapter — it converts the interface
D. Bridge — it splits abstraction from implementation

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Decorator — it adds new client-facing behaviour while preserving the interface

RATIONALE:
Adding new client-facing responsibility while keeping the component interface is Decorator, distinct from Proxy’s access-control intent.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

Competency ID: dc-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Decorator lets you ___.

OPTIONS:
A. guarantee a single instance
B. add responsibilities to individual objects dynamically without affecting other objects
C. convert one interface to another
D. share intrinsic state across objects

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. add responsibilities to individual objects dynamically without affecting other objects

RATIONALE:
Decorator adds responsibilities to individual objects at runtime. The distractors are Singleton, Adapter, and Flyweight.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: structural-decorator:B2
Paired Question ID: structural-decorator:A2
Competency ID: dc-stack
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What must a decorator share with the object it wraps?

OPTIONS:
A. A common base data structure
B. A single global instance
C. The same abstract interface, so it is interchangeable with the wrapped object
D. A different interface to force conversion

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. The same abstract interface, so it is interchangeable with the wrapped object

RATIONALE:
Sharing the component interface is what lets a decorated object be used anywhere the undecorated one is, and lets wrappers nest.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: structural-decorator:B3
Paired Question ID: structural-decorator:A3
Competency ID: dc-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A text view should optionally gain scrollbars and borders in any combination chosen at runtime, all usable wherever a plain view is expected. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Facade
B. Adapter
C. Proxy
D. Decorator

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Decorator

RATIONALE:
Runtime-composable, interchangeable add-ons over a shared interface is Decorator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: structural-decorator:B4
Paired Question ID: structural-decorator:A4
Competency ID: dc-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Notifier { public: virtual void send(std::string) = 0; };  class Email : public Notifier { void send(std::string) override; };  class SmsDecorator : public Notifier { Notifier* wrap; public: SmsDecorator(Notifier* n) : wrap(n) {} void send(std::string m) override { wrap->send(m); sendSms(m); } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Decorator
B. Proxy
C. Composite
D. Adapter

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Decorator

RATIONALE:
A same-interface wrapper that performs the wrapped action and adds extra behaviour (an SMS) is Decorator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: structural-decorator:B5
Paired Question ID: structural-decorator:A5
Competency ID: dc-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A wrapper with the same interface only adds lazy initialization and access checks, not new client-facing features. Is “Decorator” the right classification?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — every wrapper is a Decorator
B. No — controlling access without adding new client-facing behaviour is Proxy, not Decorator
C. Yes — lazy init is a new feature, so Decorator
D. No — it must be an Adapter

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. No — controlling access without adding new client-facing behaviour is Proxy, not Decorator

RATIONALE:
Access control/lazy loading without new responsibilities is Proxy intent; Decorator specifically adds new client-facing behaviour.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

A. Attaches additional responsibilities to an object dynamically, as a flexible alternative to subclassing.
B. Restricts a class to one instance.

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: structural-facade:A1
Paired Question ID: structural-facade:B1
Competency ID: fc-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Facade pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Provide a single, simplified interface to a larger body of code or a complex subsystem
B. Convert one interface to another
C. Ensure a single instance
D. Add responsibilities by wrapping

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Provide a single, simplified interface to a larger body of code or a complex subsystem

RATIONALE:
Facade offers a simplified entry point to a subsystem. The distractors are Adapter, Singleton, and Decorator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: structural-facade:A2
Paired Question ID: structural-facade:B2
Competency ID: fc-access
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Does a Facade hide or remove the subsystem’s classes?

OPTIONS:
A. It permanently removes the subsystem classes
B. It provides a simpler entry point while the subsystem classes remain accessible for advanced use
C. It converts each subsystem class’s interface
D. It guarantees only one subsystem object exists

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. It provides a simpler entry point while the subsystem classes remain accessible for advanced use

RATIONALE:
A Facade adds a convenient interface but does not forbid direct use of the subsystem when needed.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: structural-facade:A3
Paired Question ID: structural-facade:B3
Competency ID: fc-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Clients must start a video conversion with one easy call, while behind it many components (codec, audio mixer, muxer) cooperate. Which pattern offers the simple front door?

OPTIONS:
A. Adapter
B. Proxy
C. Facade
D. Bridge

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Facade

RATIONALE:
A single simplified entry point over a multi-part subsystem is Facade. The distractors convert, control access, or split abstraction.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: structural-facade:A4
Paired Question ID: structural-facade:B4
Competency ID: fc-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class ComputerStarter { CPU cpu; Memory mem; Disk disk; public: void boot() { cpu.freeze(); mem.load(0, disk.read(0)); cpu.jump(0); cpu.execute(); } };  A single class offering one method that orchestrates several subsystem objects illustrates which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Mediator
B. Composite
C. Adapter
D. Facade

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Facade

RATIONALE:
One class wrapping a multi-step subsystem interaction behind a simple method (boot) is a Facade.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: structural-facade:A5
Paired Question ID: structural-facade:B5
Competency ID: fc-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A class exposes one boot() method that calls several subsystem objects in sequence so callers don’t deal with them directly, but the subsystems don’t communicate back through it. Best classified as?

OPTIONS:
A. Facade — it simplifies one-way access to a subsystem without mediating peer-to-peer communication
B. Mediator — any coordinator is a Mediator
C. Adapter — it converts interfaces
D. Proxy — it controls access

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Facade — it simplifies one-way access to a subsystem without mediating peer-to-peer communication

RATIONALE:
One-way simplification of subsystem access is Facade; Mediator would coordinate two-way communication among peers.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

QUESTION:
A Facade primarily ___.

OPTIONS:
A. shares intrinsic state across objects
B. offers a simple, unified interface over a complex subsystem
C. encapsulates a request as an object
D. captures and restores object state

CORRECT ANSWER:

Question 2
Question ID: structural-facade:B2
Paired Question ID: structural-facade:A2
Competency ID: fc-access
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Using a Facade, can clients still bypass it to use subsystem classes directly?

OPTIONS:
A. No — the subsystem becomes private forever
B. No — the Facade deletes the subsystem
C. Yes — a Facade adds a convenient interface without forbidding direct subsystem access
D. Only if the Facade is a Singleton

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Yes — a Facade adds a convenient interface without forbidding direct subsystem access

RATIONALE:
A Facade is additive: it simplifies common use but leaves the subsystem reachable for advanced needs.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

A home-theatre app should let users press one “watch movie” button that configures amplifier, lights, projector, and player, hiding the steps. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Decorator
B. Bridge
C. Proxy
D. Facade

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Facade

RATIONALE:
A one-call simplified front over many subsystem steps is Facade.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

QUESTION:
Examine:  class OrderProcessor { Inventory inv; Payment pay; Shipping ship; public: void place(Order o) { inv.reserve(o); pay.charge(o); ship.dispatch(o); } };  A single class offering one method that coordinates several subsystems for the client is an example of ___.

OPTIONS:
A. Facade
B. Strategy
C. Observer
D. Flyweight

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Facade

Wrapping a multi-subsystem workflow behind one method (place) is a Facade.

* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 5
Question ID: structural-facade:B5
Paired Question ID: structural-facade:A5
Competency ID: fc-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A so-called Facade not only simplifies calls but also forces all subsystem objects to communicate exclusively through it, coordinating their mutual interactions. Is “Facade” still the most precise label?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — Facade always coordinates communication
B. No — centralising mutual communication between objects is Mediator’s intent, not Facade’s
C. Yes — Facade and Mediator are identical
D. No — this is actually Adapter

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. No — centralising mutual communication between objects is Mediator’s intent, not Facade’s

RATIONALE:
Once it governs two-way communication among objects, the intent matches Mediator; Facade is strictly one-way simplification.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

CATEGORY: STRUCTURAL
MODULE: Flyweight
MODULE ID: structural-flyweight
ASSESSMENT TYPE: Formal Pre-Test — Form A
APPLICABLE BLOOM LEVELS: Remember, Understand, Apply, Analyze, Evaluate
NON-APPLICABLE LEVELS: (none)
================================================================Question 1
Question ID: structural-flyweight:A1
Paired Question ID: structural-flyweight:B1
Competency ID: fw-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Flyweight pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Convert interfaces
B. Add behaviour by wrapping
C. Share common state to support large numbers of fine-grained objects efficiently
D. Provide a surrogate for access

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Share common state to support large numbers of fine-grained objects efficiently

RATIONALE:
Flyweight minimises memory by sharing state across many similar objects. The distractors are Adapter, Decorator, and Proxy.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: structural-flyweight:A2
Paired Question ID: structural-flyweight:B2
Competency ID: fw-state
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the difference between intrinsic and extrinsic state in Flyweight?

OPTIONS:
A. Intrinsic is per-client; extrinsic is shared
B. Both are stored in the flyweight
C. Both are supplied by the client each call
D. Intrinsic state is shared and stored in the flyweight; extrinsic state is context-dependent and supplied by the client

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Intrinsic state is shared and stored in the flyweight; extrinsic state is context-dependent and supplied by the client

RATIONALE:
Intrinsic (shared) state lives in the flyweight; extrinsic (context) state is passed in by the client per operation.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: structural-flyweight:A3
Paired Question ID: structural-flyweight:B3
Competency ID: fw-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A text editor renders millions of character glyphs; each glyph’s font and shape repeat, but its position differs. How do you cut memory by sharing the repeating data?

OPTIONS:
A. Flyweight
B. Composite
C. Proxy
D. Decorator

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Flyweight

RATIONALE:
Sharing repeated intrinsic data while supplying per-use position is Flyweight. The distractors build trees, control access, or add behaviour.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: structural-flyweight:A4
Paired Question ID: structural-flyweight:B4
Competency ID: fw-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Glyph { char c; Font* font; public: void draw(int x, int y); };  Glyph* GlyphFactory::get(char c) { if (!pool.count(c)) pool[c] = new Glyph(c); return pool[c]; }  Position (x, y) is passed to draw(). Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Prototype
B. Flyweight
C. Singleton
D. Adapter

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Flyweight

RATIONALE:
A factory pooling shared instances by intrinsic key (char/font) while position is passed in (extrinsic) is Flyweight.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: structural-flyweight:A5
Paired Question ID: structural-flyweight:B5
Competency ID: fw-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A “Flyweight” stores each object’s unique screen position as a member and never shares instances, creating one object per character. Does this achieve Flyweight’s goal?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — Flyweight stores all state internally
B. Yes — one object per character is the goal
C. No — keeping per-instance extrinsic state and not sharing defeats Flyweight; position should be extrinsic and instances shared
D. No — Flyweight forbids a factory

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. No — keeping per-instance extrinsic state and not sharing defeats Flyweight; position should be extrinsic and instances shared

RATIONALE:
Flyweight’s memory savings come from sharing instances and externalising context state; storing position per instance and not sharing negates the benefit.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

QUESTION:
Flyweight is used to ___.

OPTIONS:
A. encapsulate a request as an object
B. define interchangeable algorithms
C. traverse a collection
D. support large numbers of similar objects efficiently by sharing their common state

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. support large numbers of similar objects efficiently by sharing their common state

RATIONALE:
Flyweight supports many similar objects by sharing common state. The distractors are Command, Strategy, and Iterator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: structural-flyweight:B2
Paired Question ID: structural-flyweight:A2
Competency ID: fw-state
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Where is extrinsic state kept in a Flyweight design?

OPTIONS:
A. Outside the flyweight — supplied by the client or context when an operation runs
B. Permanently inside each shared flyweight
C. In a single global Singleton only
D. Nowhere — extrinsic state is discarded

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Outside the flyweight — supplied by the client or context when an operation runs

RATIONALE:
Extrinsic state is context-dependent and provided by the client at call time, so it must not be stored in the shared flyweight.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: structural-flyweight:B3
Paired Question ID: structural-flyweight:A3
Competency ID: fw-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A forest scene draws 1,000,000 trees of a few species; mesh and texture repeat per species while location varies per tree. How do you avoid storing the mesh a million times?

OPTIONS:
A. Composite
B. Flyweight
C. Bridge
D. Memento

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Flyweight

RATIONALE:
Sharing per-species intrinsic data while passing per-tree position is Flyweight.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 4

QUESTION:

OPTIONS:
A. Prototype
B. Builder
C. Flyweight
D. Proxy

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Flyweight

RATIONALE:
A factory returning shared instances keyed by intrinsic data, with position passed to render(), is Flyweight.

* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

QUESTION:
A renderer keeps one shared ParticleType (color, sprite) per kind and passes each particle’s x/y/velocity at draw time. Is this a correct Flyweight?

OPTIONS:
A. No — Flyweight must store position internally
B. No — Flyweight cannot use a factory
C. Yes — but only for fewer than 100 objects
D. Yes — sharing intrinsic data while passing extrinsic state at call time is exactly Flyweight

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Yes — sharing intrinsic data while passing extrinsic state at call time is exactly Flyweight

RATIONALE:
Shared intrinsic data plus client-supplied extrinsic state at call time is the correct Flyweight design.

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: structural-proxy:A1
Paired Question ID: structural-proxy:B1
Competency ID: px-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Proxy pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Convert one interface into another
B. Add behaviour by stacking wrappers
C. Provide a surrogate or placeholder that controls access to another object
D. Compose objects into part-whole trees

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Provide a surrogate or placeholder that controls access to another object

RATIONALE:
Proxy supplies a stand-in that controls access to a real subject. The distractors are Adapter, Decorator, and Composite.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: structural-proxy:A2
Paired Question ID: structural-proxy:B2
Competency ID: px-relate
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How does a proxy typically relate to its real subject?

OPTIONS:
A. It exposes a different interface than the subject
B. It inherits the subject’s private data directly
C. It replaces the subject permanently
D. It implements the same interface as the subject and forwards requests, optionally adding access control

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. It implements the same interface as the subject and forwards requests, optionally adding access control

RATIONALE:
A proxy shares the subject’s interface so it is interchangeable, forwarding calls while controlling access (lazy load, protection, remoting).

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: structural-proxy:A3
Paired Question ID: structural-proxy:B3
Competency ID: px-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A large image is expensive to load; you want a stand-in that exposes the same interface but defers loading until the image is actually drawn. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Proxy
B. Decorator
C. Adapter
D. Composite

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Proxy

RATIONALE:
A same-interface placeholder that delays creation of the real object is a (virtual) Proxy. The distractors add behaviour, convert, or build trees.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: structural-proxy:A4
Paired Question ID: structural-proxy:B4
Competency ID: px-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Image { public: virtual void draw() = 0; };  class ProxyImage : public Image { RealImage* real = nullptr; std::string file; public: void draw() override { if (!real) real = new RealImage(file); real->draw(); } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Adapter
B. Proxy
C. Flyweight
D. Bridge

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Proxy

RATIONALE:
Same-interface wrapper that lazily creates and delegates to the real subject is a virtual Proxy.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: structural-proxy:A5
Paired Question ID: structural-proxy:B5
Competency ID: px-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A wrapper implements the subject’s interface and adds logging plus access checks before delegating, without changing the operation’s result or adding new client-facing features. Best classified as?

OPTIONS:
A. Decorator — any wrapper that does extra work is a Decorator
B. Adapter — it converts the interface
C. Proxy — it controls access to the same operation without adding new client-facing behaviour
D. Composite — it manages children

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Proxy — it controls access to the same operation without adding new client-facing behaviour

RATIONALE:
Controlling access (auth/logging) to the same operation, without new client-facing responsibilities, is Proxy intent, distinguishing it from Decorator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: structural-proxy:B1
Paired Question ID: structural-proxy:A1
Competency ID: px-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Proxy’s core purpose is to ___.

OPTIONS:
A. share state among many fine-grained objects
B. define a family of interchangeable algorithms
C. simplify a subsystem behind one interface
D. act as a controlled stand-in for another object

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. act as a controlled stand-in for another object

RATIONALE:
Proxy is a controlled surrogate for another object. The distractors are Flyweight, Strategy, and Facade.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: structural-proxy:B2
Paired Question ID: structural-proxy:A2
Competency ID: px-relate
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What distinguishes a Proxy from a Decorator in intent?

OPTIONS:
A. A Proxy controls or manages access to the subject, while a Decorator adds new responsibilities
B. They are identical in intent
C. A Proxy always adds new features
D. A Decorator changes the object’s interface

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. A Proxy controls or manages access to the subject, while a Decorator adds new responsibilities

RATIONALE:
Both wrap a same-interface object, but Proxy’s intent is access control/management whereas Decorator’s is adding behaviour.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: structural-proxy:B3
Paired Question ID: structural-proxy:A3
Competency ID: px-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Remote service calls are costly; you want a local object with the same interface that transparently handles the connection and caches results. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Flyweight
B. Proxy
C. Adapter
D. Mediator

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Proxy

RATIONALE:
A same-interface local stand-in for a remote object (managing connection/caching) is a remote Proxy.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: structural-proxy:B4
Paired Question ID: structural-proxy:A4
Competency ID: px-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class BankAccount { public: virtual void withdraw(int) = 0; };  class ProtectedAccount : public BankAccount { RealAccount acc; User u; public: void withdraw(int n) override { if (u.isOwner()) acc.withdraw(n); } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Decorator
B. Bridge
C. Proxy
D. Strategy

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Proxy

RATIONALE:
A same-interface wrapper that performs an access check before delegating is a protection Proxy.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: structural-proxy:B5
Paired Question ID: structural-proxy:A5
Competency ID: px-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A class wraps a Database with the same interface and simply forwards every call unchanged — no access control, lazy initialization, or remoting. Is “Proxy” the right label?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — any same-interface wrapper is a Proxy
B. Yes — forwarding is the whole point
C. No — Proxy requires changing the interface
D. No — with no access control, lazy loading, or remoting it serves no proxy purpose

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. No — with no access control, lazy loading, or remoting it serves no proxy purpose

RATIONALE:
A pure pass-through adds none of the access-management responsibilities that define a Proxy, so the label is not meaningful here.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: structural-repository:A1
Paired Question ID: structural-repository:B1
Competency ID: rp-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the purpose of the Repository pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Provide a collection-like interface that mediates between domain logic and data storage, hiding persistence details
B. Convert one interface into another
C. Ensure a class has one instance
D. Share intrinsic state across objects

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Provide a collection-like interface that mediates between domain logic and data storage, hiding persistence details

RATIONALE:
Repository gives domain code a clean, collection-like interface over storage, hiding persistence. The distractors are Adapter, Singleton, and Flyweight.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Fowler (2002), Patterns of Enterprise Application Architecture — Repository
* Evans (2003), Domain-Driven Design

Question 2
Question ID: structural-repository:A2
Paired Question ID: structural-repository:B2
Competency ID: rp-classify
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Repository is NOT one of the original Gang-of-Four design patterns. How is it best classified?

OPTIONS:
A. As a GoF structural pattern like Adapter
B. As an enterprise/application architecture pattern for data access, outside the GoF catalog
C. As a GoF creational pattern
D. As a C++ language idiom

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. As an enterprise/application architecture pattern for data access, outside the GoF catalog

RATIONALE:
Repository comes from enterprise/application architecture (Fowler, Evans), not the GoF catalog; it is an application-architecture data-access pattern.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Fowler (2002), Patterns of Enterprise Application Architecture — Repository
* Evans (2003), Domain-Driven Design

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: structural-repository:A3
Paired Question ID: structural-repository:B3
Competency ID: rp-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Domain code should query and save User objects without knowing whether they live in SQL, a file, or an in-memory store, so the data source can be swapped freely. Which approach fits?

OPTIONS:
A. Singleton access
B. Decorator wrapping
C. A Repository abstraction over storage
D. A Facade over the UI

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. A Repository abstraction over storage

RATIONALE:
A domain-facing interface that hides and allows swapping the storage mechanism is the Repository approach.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Fowler (2002), Patterns of Enterprise Application Architecture — Repository
* Evans (2003), Domain-Driven Design

QUESTION:
A class named UserRepository builds SQL strings throughout the domain layer and exposes raw connection objects to callers. Does this honor the Repository idea?

OPTIONS:
A. No — Repository’s purpose is to hide persistence behind a clean interface; leaking SQL and connections to the domain defeats the separation
B. Yes — the name makes it a Repository
C. Yes — exposing connections is required
D. No — Repository must be a GoF pattern

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. No — Repository’s purpose is to hide persistence behind a clean interface; leaking SQL and connections to the domain defeats the separation

RATIONALE:
Repository exists to separate domain logic from data-access concerns; leaking SQL/connections breaks that separation regardless of the class name.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content

CATEGORY: BEHAVIOURAL

MODULE ID: behavioural-chain-of-responsibility
ASSESSMENT TYPE: Formal Pre-Test — Form A
APPLICABLE BLOOM LEVELS: Remember, Understand, Apply, Analyze, Evaluate

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: behavioural-chain-of-responsibility:A1
Paired Question ID: behavioural-chain-of-responsibility:B1
Competency ID: cor-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Chain of Responsibility pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Pass a request along a chain of handlers until one handles it, decoupling sender from receiver
B. Convert interfaces
C. Ensure one instance
D. Share intrinsic state

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Pass a request along a chain of handlers until one handles it, decoupling sender from receiver

RATIONALE:
Chain of Responsibility forwards a request through handlers until one handles it. The distractors are Adapter, Singleton, and Flyweight.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-chain-of-responsibility:A2
Paired Question ID: behavioural-chain-of-responsibility:B2
Competency ID: cor-decision
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What does each handler in the chain decide?

OPTIONS:
A. Which observers to notify
B. Whether to handle the request itself or pass it to the next handler
C. How to convert the request’s interface
D. Which single instance to return

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Whether to handle the request itself or pass it to the next handler

RATIONALE:
Each handler either handles the request or forwards it down the chain.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: behavioural-chain-of-responsibility:A3
Paired Question ID: behavioural-chain-of-responsibility:B3
Competency ID: cor-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A support ticket should be tried by level-1, then level-2, then a manager, each handling it or passing it on, with the sender unaware who resolves it. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Mediator
B. Command
C. Chain of Responsibility
D. Observer

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Chain of Responsibility

RATIONALE:
Sequential handle-or-forward with sender/receiver decoupling is Chain of Responsibility.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: behavioural-chain-of-responsibility:A4
Paired Question ID: behavioural-chain-of-responsibility:B4
Competency ID: cor-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Handler { protected: Handler* next = nullptr; public: void setNext(Handler* n) { next = n; } virtual void handle(int lvl) { if (next) next->handle(lvl); } };  class Warn : public Handler { void handle(int lvl) override { if (lvl < 2) log("warn"); else Handler::handle(lvl); } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Decorator
B. Composite
C. Observer
D. Chain of Responsibility

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Chain of Responsibility

RATIONALE:
Handlers each holding a next link and choosing to handle or forward a request is Chain of Responsibility.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: behavioural-chain-of-responsibility:A5
Paired Question ID: behavioural-chain-of-responsibility:B5
Competency ID: cor-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Wrappers each hold a “next” and either consume a request or forward it; unlike a wrapper that always augments and delegates, a link may stop the request. Best classified as?

OPTIONS:
A. Chain of Responsibility — handlers conditionally handle or forward, possibly stopping the request
B. Decorator — any chain of wrappers is Decorator
C. Adapter — it converts requests
D. Strategy — it swaps algorithms

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Chain of Responsibility — handlers conditionally handle or forward, possibly stopping the request

RATIONALE:
Conditional handle-or-forward (with the option to stop) is Chain of Responsibility; a Decorator always delegates while adding behaviour.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

QUESTION:
Chain of Responsibility ___.

OPTIONS:
A. captures and restores object state
B. lets a request travel through a series of handlers until one deals with it
C. shares intrinsic state across objects
D. defines an algorithm skeleton

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. lets a request travel through a series of handlers until one deals with it

RATIONALE:
Chain of Responsibility passes a request through handlers until one handles it. The distractors are Memento, Flyweight, and Template Method.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-chain-of-responsibility:B2
Paired Question ID: behavioural-chain-of-responsibility:A2
Competency ID: cor-decision
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What coupling does Chain of Responsibility reduce?

OPTIONS:
A. Between observer and subject
B. Between leaf and composite
C. Between the request’s sender and the specific receiver, since the sender need not know which handler responds
D. Between abstraction and implementation

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Between the request’s sender and the specific receiver, since the sender need not know which handler responds

RATIONALE:
The sender is decoupled from the concrete handler that ends up servicing the request.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Difficulty: Moderate

An HTTP request passes through authentication, then rate-limiting, then logging middleware; any stage may handle/reject it or pass it on. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Visitor
B. Facade
C. Template Method
D. Chain of Responsibility

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Chain of Responsibility

RATIONALE:
A pipeline of handlers that each handle-or-forward a request is Chain of Responsibility.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 4

Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:

A. Chain of Responsibility
B. Mediator
C. Command

Linked approvers each handling within their limit or forwarding to the next is Chain of Responsibility.

Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Each validator holds a reference to the next; on failure it stops, otherwise it calls the next, and the caller doesn’t know which validator ran. Is “Chain of Responsibility” correct?

OPTIONS:
A. No — this is Decorator
B. Yes — linked handlers that each handle-or-pass while hiding the receiver from the sender is Chain of Responsibility
C. No — the sender must know the handler
D. Yes — but only with one handler

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Yes — linked handlers that each handle-or-pass while hiding the receiver from the sender is Chain of Responsibility

RATIONALE:
Linked handlers that each handle-or-forward while the sender stays unaware of the receiver is Chain of Responsibility.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

CATEGORY: BEHAVIOURAL
