================================================================Question 1
Question ID: behavioural-command:A1
Paired Question ID: behavioural-command:B1
Competency ID: cm-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Command pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Convert interfaces
B. Share intrinsic state
C. Encapsulate a request as an object, so you can parameterise, queue, log, and undo operations
D. Traverse a collection

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Encapsulate a request as an object, so you can parameterise, queue, log, and undo operations

RATIONALE:
Command reifies a request as an object enabling queuing, logging, and undo. The distractors are Adapter, Flyweight, and Iterator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-command:A2
Paired Question ID: behavioural-command:B2
Competency ID: cm-enable
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What does turning a request into a Command object enable?

OPTIONS:
A. Faster compilation
B. Automatic multithreading
C. Removing the receiver
D. Storing, queuing, and undoing operations uniformly, and decoupling the invoker from the receiver

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Storing, queuing, and undoing operations uniformly, and decoupling the invoker from the receiver

RATIONALE:
A reified request can be stored, queued, replayed, and undone, and it decouples the invoker from the concrete receiver.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: behavioural-command:A3
Paired Question ID: behavioural-command:B3
Competency ID: cm-select

Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A text editor needs undo/redo and a macro recorder; each user action must be storable, replayable, and reversible. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Command
B. Observer
C. Strategy
D. Iterator

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Command

RATIONALE:
Reifying actions so they can be stored, replayed, and undone is Command. The distractors notify, swap algorithms, or traverse.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 4

Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Command { public: virtual void execute() = 0; virtual void undo() = 0; };  class InsertText : public Command { Doc& d; std::string t; public: void execute() override { d.insert(t); } void undo() override { d.erase(t); } };  class Invoker { std::vector<Command*> history; public: void run(Command* c) { c->execute(); history.push_back(c); } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Strategy
B. Command
C. State
D. Memento

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Command

RATIONALE:
Reified requests with execute/undo, run and recorded by an invoker, is the Command pattern.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: behavioural-command:A5
Paired Question ID: behavioural-command:B5
Competency ID: cm-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A design calls receiver methods directly from the UI handler with no request object, yet claims to support undo by re-reading the UI. Is this the Command pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — any action with undo is Command
B. Yes — direct calls are Command
C. No — Command requires reifying each request as an object (with execute/undo); calling the receiver directly is not Command
D. No — Command forbids an invoker

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. No — Command requires reifying each request as an object (with execute/undo); calling the receiver directly is not Command

RATIONALE:
Command’s essence is making each request a first-class object; direct receiver calls without command objects are not Command.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: behavioural-command:B1
Paired Question ID: behavioural-command:A1
Competency ID: cm-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
The Command pattern ___.

OPTIONS:
A. shares intrinsic state
B. wraps an action and its parameters in an object so it can be executed later, queued, or undone
C. defines a one-to-many dependency
D. selects among interchangeable algorithms

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. wraps an action and its parameters in an object so it can be executed later, queued, or undone

RATIONALE:
Command wraps an action as an object for later execution, queuing, or undo. The distractors are Flyweight, Observer, and Strategy.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-command:B2
Paired Question ID: behavioural-command:A2
Competency ID: cm-enable
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How does Command decouple the invoker from the receiver?

OPTIONS:
A. The invoker triggers an abstract command and never needs to know which receiver or operation it wraps
B. The invoker inherits from the receiver
C. The receiver calls the invoker directly
D. They must be the same object

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. The invoker triggers an abstract command and never needs to know which receiver or operation it wraps

RATIONALE:
The invoker depends only on the abstract command, so it is independent of the concrete receiver and operation.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

B. Command

Configurable, queueable, undoable actions as objects is Command.

* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: behavioural-interpreter:A1
Paired Question ID: behavioural-interpreter:B1
Competency ID: in-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Interpreter pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Convert interfaces
B. Share intrinsic state
C. Given a language, define a representation for its grammar and an interpreter that uses the representation to evaluate sentences
D. Provide a surrogate for access

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Given a language, define a representation for its grammar and an interpreter that uses the representation to evaluate sentences

RATIONALE:
Interpreter represents a grammar and evaluates sentences in that language. The distractors are Adapter, Flyweight, and Proxy.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-interpreter:A2
Paired Question ID: behavioural-interpreter:B2
Competency ID: in-represent
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How are grammar rules typically represented in the Interpreter pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. As a single regex
B. As a global lookup table only
C. As one giant switch statement
D. Each grammar rule becomes a class with an interpret() method; expressions form a tree of these objects

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Each grammar rule becomes a class with an interpret() method; expressions form a tree of these objects

RATIONALE:
Each rule maps to a class with interpret(); a sentence is an abstract syntax tree of those rule objects.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: behavioural-interpreter:A3
Paired Question ID: behavioural-interpreter:B3
Competency ID: in-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
You must evaluate simple boolean expressions like (A AND B) OR C entered by users, composing rules recursively. Which pattern represents and evaluates such a grammar?

OPTIONS:
A. Interpreter
B. Visitor
C. Strategy
D. Composite

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Interpreter

RATIONALE:
Representing grammar rules as composable evaluable objects is Interpreter. The distractors add operations, swap algorithms, or only model part-whole trees.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: behavioural-interpreter:A4
Paired Question ID: behavioural-interpreter:B4
Competency ID: in-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Expr { public: virtual int eval() = 0; };  class Num : public Expr { int v; public: int eval() override { return v; } };  class Add : public Expr { Expr* l; Expr* r; public: int eval() override { return l->eval() + r->eval(); } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Composite
B. Interpreter
C. Iterator
D. Command

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Interpreter

RATIONALE:
Grammar-rule classes with eval() composed into an expression tree is the Interpreter pattern.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 5
Question ID: behavioural-interpreter:A5
Paired Question ID: behavioural-interpreter:B5
Competency ID: in-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A team uses the Interpreter pattern for a complex, performance-critical, full programming language with deeply nested grammar. Is this an appropriate use, and why?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — Interpreter scales best to large languages
B. Yes — performance is Interpreter’s strength
C. No — Interpreter fits small, simple, stable grammars; large complex ones are better served by parser/compiler tools
D. No — Interpreter cannot evaluate expressions

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. No — Interpreter fits small, simple, stable grammars; large complex ones are better served by parser/compiler tools

RATIONALE:
Interpreter suits small, stable grammars; for large/complex performance-critical languages it becomes unwieldy and a parser/compiler is preferable.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

Difficulty: Moderate

QUESTION:
What is the relationship between Interpreter and a tree of expression objects?

OPTIONS:
A. Sentences are represented as an abstract syntax tree of rule objects, and interpretation recurses over that tree
B. Interpretation requires no data structure
C. Each sentence is one flat object
D. The grammar must be stored in a database

CORRECT ANSWER:

RATIONALE:
A sentence becomes an AST of rule objects; interpret() recurses over the tree to evaluate it.

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: behavioural-iterator:A1
Paired Question ID: behavioural-iterator:B1
Competency ID: it-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Iterator pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Provide a way to access elements of a collection sequentially without exposing its underlying representation
B. Convert interfaces
C. Ensure one instance
D. Add behaviour by wrapping

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Provide a way to access elements of a collection sequentially without exposing its underlying representation

RATIONALE:
Iterator gives sequential access while hiding the collection’s internals. The distractors are Adapter, Singleton, and Decorator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-iterator:A2
Paired Question ID: behavioural-iterator:B2
Competency ID: it-rationale
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Why use an Iterator instead of exposing the collection’s internal structure?

OPTIONS:
A. It makes the collection a Singleton
B. It lets clients traverse uniformly while the collection keeps its representation private and can change it
C. It converts the element type
D. It deletes the collection after traversal

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. It lets clients traverse uniformly while the collection keeps its representation private and can change it

RATIONALE:
Iterator decouples traversal from representation, so the collection can hide and later change its internals.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: behavioural-iterator:A3
Paired Question ID: behavioural-iterator:B3
Competency ID: it-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
You want client code to walk over a custom tree and a custom hash set the same way (begin/next/done) without knowing how each stores data. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Visitor
B. Composite
C. Iterator
D. Observer

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Iterator

RATIONALE:
Uniform sequential traversal that hides each collection’s internal storage is Iterator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: behavioural-iterator:A4
Paired Question ID: behavioural-iterator:B4
Competency ID: it-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Iterator { public: virtual bool hasNext() = 0; virtual int next() = 0; };  class ListIterator : public Iterator { std::vector<int>& v; size_t i = 0; public: bool hasNext() override { return i < v.size(); } int next() override { return v[i++]; } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Strategy
B. Command
C. State
D. Iterator

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Iterator

RATIONALE:
An object exposing hasNext/next while tracking position and hiding the container is the Iterator pattern.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 5
Question ID: behavioural-iterator:A5
Paired Question ID: behavioural-iterator:B5
Competency ID: it-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A “traversal” design forces clients to read the collection’s internal array index and bounds directly to loop. Does this satisfy Iterator’s intent?

OPTIONS:
A. No — exposing internal representation to clients defeats Iterator’s purpose
B. Yes — direct index access is the Iterator pattern
C. Yes — as long as there is a loop
D. No — Iterator forbids sequential access

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. No — exposing internal representation to clients defeats Iterator’s purpose

RATIONALE:
Iterator exists to hide the representation; making clients use internal indices breaks the encapsulation it provides.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: behavioural-iterator:B1

Competency ID: it-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy

QUESTION:
Iterator provides ___.

OPTIONS:
A. a single global instance
B. sequential access to a collection’s elements without revealing its internal structure
C. a one-to-many notification
D. a simplified subsystem interface

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. sequential access to a collection’s elements without revealing its internal structure

RATIONALE:
Iterator gives sequential element access while hiding internals. The distractors are Singleton, Observer, and Facade.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-iterator:B2
Paired Question ID: behavioural-iterator:A2
Competency ID: it-rationale
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What responsibility moves into the iterator object?

OPTIONS:
A. Owning all the collection’s data
B. Converting element interfaces
C. Tracking the current traversal position, so multiple independent traversals can coexist
D. Notifying observers of changes

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Tracking the current traversal position, so multiple independent traversals can coexist

RATIONALE:
The iterator holds the traversal cursor, which allows several simultaneous, independent traversals of one collection.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: behavioural-iterator:B3
Paired Question ID: behavioural-iterator:A3
Competency ID: it-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A library exposes several container types; users should loop through any of them with the same code, and even run two simultaneous passes over one container. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Memento
B. Facade
C. Bridge
D. Iterator

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Iterator

RATIONALE:
Uniform traversal plus independent concurrent passes (separate cursors) is Iterator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 4
Question ID: behavioural-iterator:B4
Paired Question ID: behavioural-iterator:A4
Competency ID: it-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class TreeIterator { std::stack<Node*> st; public: TreeIterator(Node* root) { if (root) st.push(root); } bool done() { return st.empty(); } Node* current() { return st.top(); } void advance() { Node* n = st.top(); st.pop(); if (n->right) st.push(n->right); if (n->left) st.push(n->left); } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Iterator
B. Visitor
C. Composite
D. Strategy

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Iterator

RATIONALE:
An object that maintains traversal state and exposes done/current/advance over a structure is an Iterator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

A collection returns a cursor object exposing hasNext()/next() and keeps its storage (array vs linked list) private and swappable. Is “Iterator” a defensible label?

OPTIONS:
A. No — this must be Strategy
B. Yes — a cursor giving sequential access while the collection hides its representation is exactly Iterator
C. No — Iterator must expose the storage
D. Yes — but only for arrays

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Yes — a cursor giving sequential access while the collection hides its representation is exactly Iterator

RATIONALE:
A cursor giving sequential access while the underlying storage stays private and changeable is the Iterator pattern.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Difficulty: Easy

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Mediator pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Convert interfaces
B. Traverse a collection
C. Define an object that centralises and coordinates communication between a set of objects so they don’t refer to each other directly
D. Share intrinsic state

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Define an object that centralises and coordinates communication between a set of objects so they don’t refer to each other directly

RATIONALE:
Mediator centralises inter-object communication. The distractors are Adapter, Iterator, and Flyweight.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-mediator:A2
Paired Question ID: behavioural-mediator:B2
Competency ID: md-coupling
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How does a Mediator reduce coupling among colleague objects?

OPTIONS:
A. By merging all colleagues into one class
B. By making each colleague a Singleton
C. By converting their interfaces
D. Colleagues talk only to the mediator, which routes interactions, instead of holding references to each other

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Colleagues talk only to the mediator, which routes interactions, instead of holding references to each other

RATIONALE:
Colleagues communicate through the mediator rather than referencing each other, cutting many-to-many coupling.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: behavioural-mediator:A3
Paired Question ID: behavioural-mediator:B3
Competency ID: md-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A dialog’s many widgets (list, text box, button) must enable/disable each other in complex ways; you want to avoid each widget referencing all the others. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Mediator
B. Observer
C. Composite
D. Command

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Mediator

RATIONALE:
Centralising the widgets’ mutual interactions in one coordinator is Mediator. Observer would be one-way notification.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question ID: behavioural-mediator:A4

Competency ID: md-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Mediator { public: virtual void notify(Widget* sender, std::string ev) = 0; };  class Dialog : public Mediator { Button* ok; ListBox* list; public: void notify(Widget* s, std::string ev) override { if (s == list && ev == "select") ok->enable(); } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Observer
B. Mediator
C. Facade
D. Visitor

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Mediator

RATIONALE:
A hub that receives events from colleagues and coordinates how others respond is Mediator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Paired Question ID: behavioural-mediator:B5
Competency ID: md-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A hub receives events from peer objects and tells other peers how to react, governing their mutual interaction (not one-way notification, not just simplifying a subsystem). Best classified as?

OPTIONS:
A. Observer — it is one-to-many notification
B. Facade — it just simplifies a subsystem
C. Mediator — it centralises and coordinates mutual communication among peers
D. Adapter — it converts interfaces

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Mediator — it centralises and coordinates mutual communication among peers

RATIONALE:
Two-way coordination of peers’ mutual interactions is Mediator; Observer is one-way, Facade is one-way subsystem simplification.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Memento pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Capture and externalise an object’s internal state so it can be restored later, without violating encapsulation
B. Convert interfaces
C. Ensure one instance
D. Share intrinsic state across objects

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Capture and externalise an object’s internal state so it can be restored later, without violating encapsulation

RATIONALE:
Memento captures and restores state without breaking encapsulation. The distractors are Adapter, Singleton, and Flyweight.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-memento:A2
Paired Question ID: behavioural-memento:B2
Competency ID: mm-encaps
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How does Memento preserve encapsulation while saving state?

OPTIONS:
A. It makes all fields public
B. The originator creates an opaque memento that the caretaker stores without inspecting its internals
C. It copies the originator’s class definition
D. It exposes the state through getters to everyone

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. The originator creates an opaque memento that the caretaker stores without inspecting its internals

RATIONALE:
The originator builds the memento; the caretaker stores it opaquely, never reading the internals, so encapsulation holds.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------

A. Command
B. State
C. Memento
D. Observer

C. Memento

RATIONALE:
Snapshotting and restoring state without exposing internals is Memento. Command reifies requests; State models modes.

* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Snapshot { friend class Game; int hp; int level; Snapshot(int h, int l) : hp(h), level(l) {} };  class Game { int hp; int level; public: Snapshot save() { return Snapshot(hp, level); } void load(const Snapshot& s) { hp = s.hp; level = s.level; } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Memento
B. Command
C. Observer
D. Strategy

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Memento

RATIONALE:
An originator producing/consuming an opaque state snapshot is the Memento pattern.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: behavioural-memento:B5
Paired Question ID: behavioural-memento:A5
Competency ID: mm-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
An object returns an opaque token capturing its current state; a history manager stores tokens and hands one back to restore the object, never reading the token’s contents. Is “Memento” correct?

OPTIONS:
A. No — this is Command
B. Yes — an opaque state object stored by a caretaker and used to restore the object, without inspection, is Memento
C. No — Memento requires public state
D. Yes — but only for games

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Yes — an opaque state object stored by a caretaker and used to restore the object, without inspection, is Memento

RATIONALE:
An opaque snapshot held by a caretaker and used to restore the originator, without inspection, is the Memento pattern.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

CATEGORY: BEHAVIOURAL

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: behavioural-observer:A1
Paired Question ID: behavioural-observer:B1
Competency ID: ob-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Observer pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Convert interfaces
B. Share intrinsic state
C. Define a one-to-many dependency so that when one object changes state, its dependents are notified automatically
D. Encapsulate a request as an object

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Define a one-to-many dependency so that when one object changes state, its dependents are notified automatically

RATIONALE:
Observer establishes one-to-many automatic notification. The distractors are Adapter, Flyweight, and Command.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-observer:A2
Paired Question ID: behavioural-observer:B2
Competency ID: ob-mechanism
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How do observers learn about changes in the subject?

OPTIONS:
A. Observers poll a global variable
B. Observers inherit the subject’s data
C. Each observer edits the subject directly
D. The subject holds a list of observers and calls their update method when its state changes

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. The subject holds a list of observers and calls their update method when its state changes

RATIONALE:
The subject keeps registered observers and notifies them (calls update) on change; observers do not poll or inherit.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: behavioural-observer:A3
Paired Question ID: behavioural-observer:B3
Competency ID: ob-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A spreadsheet cell’s value changes and several charts plus a status bar must refresh automatically, without the cell knowing their concrete types. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Observer
B. Mediator
C. Command
D. Strategy

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Observer

RATIONALE:
Automatic one-to-many notification with loose coupling to dependents is Observer. The distractors coordinate peers, encapsulate requests, or swap algorithms.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: behavioural-observer:A4
Paired Question ID: behavioural-observer:B4
Competency ID: ob-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Observer { public: virtual void update(int) = 0; };  class Subject { std::vector<Observer*> obs; int v; public: void attach(Observer* o) { obs.push_back(o); } void set(int x) { v = x; for (auto* o : obs) o->update(v); } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Mediator
B. Observer
C. Visitor
D. Iterator

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Observer

RATIONALE:
A subject maintaining observers and broadcasting state changes to them is the Observer pattern.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: behavioural-observer:A5
Paired Question ID: behavioural-observer:B5
Competency ID: ob-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Each dependent registers with one source and is notified when that source changes; the dependents do not coordinate each other’s mutual interactions. Best classified as?

OPTIONS:
A. Mediator — any notification is Mediator
B. Command — it encapsulates requests
C. Observer — it is one-to-many change notification, not centralised peer coordination
D. State — behaviour changes with state

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Observer — it is one-to-many change notification, not centralised peer coordination

RATIONALE:
One-to-many change notification is Observer; Mediator would centralise two-way coordination among peers.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-observer:B2
Paired Question ID: behavioural-observer:A2
Competency ID: ob-mechanism
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Why does the subject depend only on an observer interface, not concrete observers?

OPTIONS:
A. So new observer types can subscribe without modifying the subject (loose coupling)
B. So the subject can delete observers’ data
C. Because observers must be Singletons
D. So the subject can convert their interfaces

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. So new observer types can subscribe without modifying the subject (loose coupling)

RATIONALE:
Depending on an abstract observer interface lets new observers subscribe without changing the subject — the loose coupling Observer provides.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

B. Observer

Dynamic, extensible one-to-many notification is Observer.

* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Dynamic subscription plus broadcast notification of changes is the Observer pattern.

Paired Question ID: behavioural-state:B1

Difficulty: Easy

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the State pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Convert interfaces
B. Share intrinsic state
C. Allow an object to alter its behaviour when its internal state changes, appearing to change its class
D. Traverse a collection

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Allow an object to alter its behaviour when its internal state changes, appearing to change its class

RATIONALE:
State lets an object change behaviour as its internal state changes. The distractors are Adapter, Flyweight, and Iterator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-state:A2
Paired Question ID: behavioural-state:B2
Competency ID: stt-mechanism
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How does the State pattern avoid large conditional blocks on a status field?

OPTIONS:
A. By using one giant switch statement
B. By making the object a Singleton
C. By copying the object per state
D. By representing each state as an object and delegating behaviour to the current state, which can switch to another

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. By representing each state as an object and delegating behaviour to the current state, which can switch to another

RATIONALE:
Each state becomes an object encapsulating its behaviour and transitions; the context delegates to the current one.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: behavioural-state:A3
Paired Question ID: behavioural-state:B3
Competency ID: stt-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A TCP connection behaves differently when Closed, Listening, or Established, and each event may move it to another mode with its own rules. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. State
B. Strategy
C. Observer
D. Command

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. State

RATIONALE:
Behaviour and transitions driven by the object’s changing mode is State. Strategy is client-chosen and not transition-driven.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: behavioural-state:A4
Paired Question ID: behavioural-state:B4
Competency ID: stt-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class State { public: virtual void insertCoin(Machine&) = 0; };  class NoCoin : public State { void insertCoin(Machine& m) override { m.setState(new HasCoin()); } };  class Machine { State* s; public: void setState(State* x) { s = x; } void insertCoin() { s->insertCoin(*this); } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Strategy
B. State
C. Command
D. Mediator

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. State

RATIONALE:
Behaviour delegated to a current-state object that itself triggers the transition to another state is the State pattern.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 5
Question ID: behavioural-state:A5
Paired Question ID: behavioural-state:B5
Competency ID: stt-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Behaviour is delegated to an interchangeable object, and that object itself decides to replace the current one based on events, driving transitions. Is this State or Strategy?

OPTIONS:
A. Strategy — any delegation is Strategy
B. Observer — it notifies dependents
C. State — the behaviour objects drive transitions based on the object’s changing state
D. Template Method — it fixes a skeleton

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. State — the behaviour objects drive transitions based on the object’s changing state

RATIONALE:
When the delegated objects themselves drive transitions based on the object’s evolving state, it is State, not a client-chosen Strategy.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

OPTIONS:
A. They share a shape (delegate to an interface); State transitions between states internally, Strategy is chosen by the client.
B. State guarantees one instance.
C. State clones prototypes.
D. They are unrelated.

CORRECT ANSWER: A. They share a shape (delegate to an interface); State transitions between states internally, Strategy is chosen by the client.

What is the intent of the Strategy pattern?

Question 2

Paired Question ID: behavioural-strategy:B2
Competency ID: st-mechanism
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How does Strategy let the algorithm change without changing the client?

OPTIONS:
A. The client edits the algorithm’s code each time
B. The client depends on a strategy interface and delegates, so concrete algorithms swap freely
C. The client inherits each algorithm
D. The algorithms share one global instance

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. The client depends on a strategy interface and delegates, so concrete algorithms swap freely

RATIONALE:
The client holds a strategy interface and delegates; concrete strategies are interchangeable behind it.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Paired Question ID: behavioural-strategy:B3

Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A checkout must compute shipping cost by several methods (flat, weight-based, distance-based), chosen per order at runtime, without if/else sprawl. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Observer
B. Template Method
C. Strategy
D. State

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Strategy

RATIONALE:
Selecting among interchangeable algorithms at runtime is Strategy. Template Method fixes a skeleton; State is driven by internal state; Observer notifies.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

QUESTION:
A design swaps an object’s algorithm via an interface, but the object never changes which one it uses based on its own internal state — the client picks it. Is Strategy or State the better fit?

OPTIONS:
A. Strategy — it is a client-selected interchangeable algorithm, not state-driven behaviour
B. State — any swappable behaviour is State
C. Observer — it notifies dependents
D. Command — it encapsulates a request

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Strategy — it is a client-selected interchangeable algorithm, not state-driven behaviour

Strategy is a client-chosen algorithm; State changes behaviour as the object’s own state transitions. Here the client selects it, so Strategy fits.

QUESTION:
What is the relationship between context and strategy?

OPTIONS:
A. The strategy owns and controls the context
B. Context and strategy are the same object
C. The context delegates the varying behaviour to a strategy object it holds
D. The strategy must be a Singleton

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. The context delegates the varying behaviour to a strategy object it holds

RATIONALE:
The context delegates the variable behaviour to a held strategy, so swapping strategies changes behaviour without touching the context.

* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

================================================================Question 1

Paired Question ID: behavioural-template-method:B1
Competency ID: tm-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Template Method pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Define the skeleton of an algorithm in a base method, deferring some steps to subclasses
B. Convert interfaces
C. Ensure one instance
D. Share intrinsic state

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Define the skeleton of an algorithm in a base method, deferring some steps to subclasses

RATIONALE:
Template Method fixes an algorithm’s skeleton and lets subclasses supply steps. The distractors are Adapter, Singleton, and Flyweight.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-template-method:A2
Paired Question ID: behavioural-template-method:B2
Competency ID: tm-fixedvary
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
In Template Method, what stays fixed and what varies?

OPTIONS:
A. Everything is overridden
B. The overall step sequence is fixed in the base class while specific steps are overridden by subclasses
C. Nothing can be overridden
D. The client supplies the whole algorithm at runtime

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. The overall step sequence is fixed in the base class while specific steps are overridden by subclasses

RATIONALE:
The invariant sequence lives in the base method; subclasses override only the designated variable steps.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------

Question ID: behavioural-template-method:A3

Difficulty: Moderate

QUESTION:
Several report generators share the same overall flow (open → fetch → format → save) but differ only in how they format. You want the flow defined once. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Strategy
B. Command
C. Template Method
D. Observer

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Template Method

RATIONALE:
A fixed algorithm skeleton with a varying step implemented per subclass is Template Method. Strategy would inject the whole algorithm as an object.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

A. Strategy
B. State
C. Command
D. Template Method

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Template Method

A non-virtual base method fixing the flow and calling overridable steps is Template Method.

QUESTION:
An algorithm’s invariant steps live in a base method that calls overridable hooks; subclasses customise only those hooks. Is this Template Method or Strategy?

OPTIONS:
A. Template Method — variation happens via subclass overrides of fixed hook steps (inheritance), not a swapped strategy object
B. Strategy — any variation is Strategy
C. Observer — it notifies dependents
D. Composite — it forms a tree

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Template Method — variation happens via subclass overrides of fixed hook steps (inheritance), not a swapped strategy object

RATIONALE:
Template Method varies behaviour through subclass overrides of fixed hooks (inheritance); Strategy varies it by composing a swappable object.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

What is the intent of the Visitor pattern?

B. Convert interfaces
C. Ensure one instance
D. Traverse a collection sequentially

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Represent an operation to perform on the elements of an object structure, letting you add new operations without changing the element classes

RATIONALE:
Visitor adds operations over a structure without modifying its element classes. The distractors are Adapter, Singleton, and Iterator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-visitor:A2
Paired Question ID: behavioural-visitor:B2
Competency ID: vs-mechanism
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How does Visitor add a new operation across a class hierarchy without editing those classes?

OPTIONS:
A. By rewriting every element class
B. Each element accepts a visitor and dispatches to the visitor’s method for its type; new operations are new visitor classes
C. By making each element a Singleton
D. By converting each element’s interface

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Each element accepts a visitor and dispatches to the visitor’s method for its type; new operations are new visitor classes

RATIONALE:
Elements accept() a visitor and call back the type-specific visit() (double dispatch); a new operation is simply a new visitor.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: behavioural-visitor:A3
Paired Question ID: behavioural-visitor:B3
Competency ID: vs-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
An AST has many node types; you must add operations like type-check, pretty-print, and optimise without modifying each node class every time. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Composite
B. Iterator
C. Visitor
D. Strategy

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Visitor

RATIONALE:
Adding many operations over a stable element hierarchy without editing elements is Visitor.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: behavioural-visitor:A4
Paired Question ID: behavioural-visitor:B4
Competency ID: vs-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Visitor; class Node { public: virtual void accept(Visitor&) = 0; };  class Number : public Node { public: int v; void accept(Visitor& vi) override; /* vi.visit(*this) */ };  class Visitor { public: virtual void visit(Number&) = 0; virtual void visit(Add&) = 0; };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Composite
B. Mediator
C. Observer
D. Visitor

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Visitor

RATIONALE:
accept()/visit() double dispatch over an element hierarchy is the Visitor pattern.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: behavioural-visitor:A5
Paired Question ID: behavioural-visitor:B5
Competency ID: vs-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A team needs to add many unrelated operations over a stable set of node types and rarely adds new node types. Is Visitor an appropriate choice, and why?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — Visitor fits stable element types with frequently-added operations, localising each operation in a visitor (though adding new element types is costly)
B. No — Visitor is for adding new element types easily
C. No — Visitor cannot support multiple operations
D. Yes — because Visitor makes adding new node types trivial

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Yes — Visitor fits stable element types with frequently-added operations, localising each operation in a visitor (though adding new element types is costly)

RATIONALE:
Visitor suits stable element hierarchies with many added operations; its known cost is that new element types require touching every visitor.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

Question 2

Paired Question ID: behavioural-visitor:A2
Competency ID: vs-mechanism
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What technique lets the correct visitor method run for each concrete element type?

OPTIONS:
A. A global switch on a type tag
B. Reflection at link time
C. Double dispatch — accept() on the element calls the visitor overload specific to that element’s type
D. Copying the element first

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Double dispatch — accept() on the element calls the visitor overload specific to that element’s type

RATIONALE:
Double dispatch (element.accept → visitor.visit overload) selects the right operation for the concrete element type.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 5

Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Adding a new node type to a working Visitor design forces edits to every visitor class. Is this a known and acceptable trade-off of Visitor?

OPTIONS:
A. No — Visitor makes adding element types free
B. Yes — Visitor trades cheap new operations for costly new element types; this is its well-known trade-off, acceptable when element types are stable
C. No — it means the design is wrong
D. Yes — but only in C++

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Yes — Visitor trades cheap new operations for costly new element types; this is its well-known trade-off, acceptable when element types are stable

RATIONALE:
Visitor’s recognised trade-off: easy new operations, costly new element types — acceptable when the element set is stable.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

CATEGORY: IDIOMS
MODULE: PIMPL (Pointer to Implementation)
MODULE ID: idioms-pimpl
ASSESSMENT TYPE: Formal Pre-Test — Form A
APPLICABLE BLOOM LEVELS: Remember, Understand, Apply, Analyze, Evaluate

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: idioms-pimpl:A1
Paired Question ID: idioms-pimpl:B1
Competency ID: pimpl-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What does the PIMPL (pointer to implementation) idiom do?

OPTIONS:
A. Converts one interface into another
B. Shares intrinsic state across many objects
C. Hides a class’s private members behind a pointer to an implementation struct, keeping the header stable
D. Provides one-to-many notification

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Hides a class’s private members behind a pointer to an implementation struct, keeping the header stable

RATIONALE:
PIMPL moves private members into a hidden implementation accessed through a pointer, so the header stays stable. The distractors are Adapter, Flyweight, and Observer.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Sutter (1999), Exceptional C++ — Compilation Firewalls (PIMPL)
* CodiNeo Idioms module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: idioms-pimpl:A2
Paired Question ID: idioms-pimpl:B2
Competency ID: pimpl-classify
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How should PIMPL be classified relative to the GoF design patterns?

OPTIONS:
A. As the GoF Bridge pattern
B. As a GoF structural pattern
C. As a GoF creational pattern
D. As a C++ implementation idiom (a compilation firewall), distinct from Bridge

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. As a C++ implementation idiom (a compilation firewall), distinct from Bridge

RATIONALE:
PIMPL is a C++ implementation idiom for header insulation, not a GoF pattern, and is distinct from Bridge (which varies an abstraction/implementation hierarchy).

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Sutter (1999), Exceptional C++ — Compilation Firewalls (PIMPL)
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: idioms-pimpl:A3
Paired Question ID: idioms-pimpl:B3
Competency ID: pimpl-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A widely-included library header keeps changing its private members, forcing slow recompiles of every client. You want the header to stop exposing those members so clients rarely recompile. Which technique fits?

OPTIONS:
A. The PIMPL idiom (pointer to a hidden implementation)
B. The Composite pattern
C. The Observer pattern
D. The Strategy pattern

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. The PIMPL idiom (pointer to a hidden implementation)

RATIONALE:
Insulating clients from private-member changes via a hidden implementation pointer is PIMPL. The distractors solve unrelated problems.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Sutter (1999), Exceptional C++ — Compilation Firewalls (PIMPL)

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: idioms-pimpl:A4
Paired Question ID: idioms-pimpl:B4
Competency ID: pimpl-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine (widget.h):  class Widget { public: Widget(); ~Widget(); void draw(); private: struct Impl; std::unique_ptr<Impl> p; };  with all members defined in widget.cpp. Which C++ idiom does this header demonstrate?

OPTIONS:
A. Bridge pattern
B. PIMPL idiom
C. Flyweight pattern
D. Proxy pattern

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. PIMPL idiom

RATIONALE:
An opaque nested Impl held by a smart pointer, with definitions in the .cpp, is the PIMPL idiom (a compilation firewall).

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Sutter (1999), Exceptional C++ — Compilation Firewalls (PIMPL)
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: idioms-pimpl:A5
Paired Question ID: idioms-pimpl:B5
Competency ID: pimpl-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A developer says a class “uses Bridge” because it holds a unique_ptr<Impl>. But there is exactly one Impl type whose only purpose is to hide private members and reduce header dependencies — no abstraction/implementation hierarchy varies. Is “Bridge” accurate?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — any pointer-to-impl is Bridge
B. Yes — Bridge and PIMPL are interchangeable terms
C. No — a single fixed implementation used only to hide members and stabilise the header is the PIMPL idiom, not Bridge
D. No — this is actually the Adapter pattern

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. No — a single fixed implementation used only to hide members and stabilise the header is the PIMPL idiom, not Bridge

RATIONALE:
With one fixed implementation used solely for header insulation, this is PIMPL, not Bridge — Bridge requires independently varying abstraction and implementation hierarchies.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Sutter (1999), Exceptional C++ — Compilation Firewalls (PIMPL)
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

================================================================Question 1

Paired Question ID: idioms-pimpl:A1
Competency ID: pimpl-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
The PIMPL idiom primarily ___.

OPTIONS:
A. shares intrinsic state across objects
B. defines interchangeable algorithms
C. captures and restores object state
D. moves a class’s private implementation behind a pointer so the header stays stable and dependencies shrink

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. moves a class’s private implementation behind a pointer so the header stays stable and dependencies shrink

RATIONALE:
PIMPL relocates the private implementation behind a pointer to insulate the header and cut dependencies. The distractors are Flyweight, Strategy, and Memento.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Sutter (1999), Exceptional C++ — Compilation Firewalls (PIMPL)
* CodiNeo Idioms module content

Which benefits does PIMPL provide?

C. Automatic object pooling
D. One-to-many event notification

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Reduced header/compile-time dependencies, a more stable header/ABI, and hidden implementation details

RATIONALE:
PIMPL’s benefits are fewer header dependencies, a more stable header/ABI, and hidden implementation details. The distractors describe Strategy, Flyweight, and Observer.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Sutter (1999), Exceptional C++ — Compilation Firewalls (PIMPL)
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

QUESTION:
A class exposes only public methods in its header and forwards every call to a privately-pointed Impl defined in the source file, with one Impl type whose sole job is hiding members. Is calling this “PIMPL” (not Bridge) correct?

OPTIONS:
A. No — it must be called Bridge
B. No — PIMPL requires multiple implementations
C. Yes — but only if it is in the standard library
D. Yes — a single hidden implementation behind a pointer to insulate the header is PIMPL, distinct from Bridge’s varying abstraction/implementation hierarchies

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Yes — a single hidden implementation behind a pointer to insulate the header is PIMPL, distinct from Bridge’s varying abstraction/implementation hierarchies

RATIONALE:
A single hidden implementation behind a pointer used to insulate the header is PIMPL; Bridge would require independently varying abstraction and implementation hierarchies.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Sutter (1999), Exceptional C++ — Compilation Firewalls (PIMPL)
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++
